(b) Adverbs

Abaza kurungi nk’abahumakati.
She speaks as sweetly as noble ladies do.

Orra muno nk’abakazi.
You cry as much as women do. (Idiomatically: stop crying like women.)

Habwaki noorubata mpora nk’omurwaire?
Why are you walking so slowly as a sick person?

Kaija ajwaha kikazi nka mukuru we.
Kaija gets tired in a woman like manner as his elder brother does.

Araire kubi nkaijo oku yaraire.
He slept as uncomfortably as he did yesterday.

By the use of the infinitive verb okwingana ‘to be equal to’, used in the

jectives

The shirt he has put on is not so white as yours.

Laimu teyera okwingana cooka.
Lime is not so white as chalk.

srbs which imply both action and manner the present-indefinite tense

uhweza runs as fast as Katabarwa does.

MPARATIVE DEGREE

s degree adjectives are compared in order to show which of the two
2 or things possesses a certain quality or state in a greater degree than
her. Adverbs are compared to show which of the two people or things

C. Comparison of adjectives and adverbs 311

did, or does, or has done, or will do something in a manner which is in a
greater degree than the other.

The forms which the verb okukira ‘to surpass, exceed’ takes in order to do
this are given below.

(1) Constructions with the infinitive

With adjectives the verb okukira must be placed between the adjective and
the noun signifying the person or thing exceeded in the degree of its quality
by another person or thing.

With adverbs it must be placed between the adverb and the noun signifying
the person or thing exceeded in the degree of the manner in which it did, or
does, or has done, or will do, something. The adverb may not be necessary if
the verb which implies both action and manner is in use, e.g.

(a) Adjectives

Muka Rwakaikara mukooto okukira muka Balinda.
Rwakaikara’s wife is bigger than Balinda’s wife.

Entale ntaito okukira embogo.
A lion is smaller than a buffalo.

In questions seeking to find out which of the two persons or things possesses
a quality in a greater degree than the other, the verb okukira may be left out.
But apart from answers to such questions, which consist of only the noun as
the subject and predicate, the rest will make use of a self-standing pronoun
to which the copula ni- has been added so as to give emphasis to something
possessing a quality in a greater degree than the other, e.g.

Mukazi ki omukooto, muka Balinda, nandiki/rundi/muka Rwakaikara?
Which is the bigger, Balinda’s or Rwakaikara’s wife?

Muka Rwakaikara.

Rwakaikara’s wife is.

Muka Rwakaikara nuwe mukooto.

Rwakaikara’s wife is the bigger.

Muka Rwakaikara nuwe mukooto okukira muka Balinda.

It is Rwakaikara’s wife that is bigger than Balinda’s wife.

(b) Adverbs

Bagonza abaza kurungi okukira Balinda.
Bagonza speaks more clearly than Balinda.

C. Comparison of adjectives and adverbs 313

Kabonesa azina okukira Kahuubire.
Kabonesa sings more beautifully than Kahuubire.

(2) Constructions with the causative

When this form of the verb okukira is in use, no adjective is ever used;
quality is expressed by an abstract noun formed from the adjective which
would otherwise have been used. To express manner, if no verb which
implies both action and manner is in use, the adverb follows a verb in the
infinitive form.

For quality the verb takes the present-imperfect tense and must be placed
between the two nouns signifying the people or things each of which has a
quality the degree of which is being compared to the other.

For manner either the present-indefinite or the present-imperfect tense is
used according to whether the action expressed is of temporary or permanent
nature. The verb okukira is here again placed between the two nouns signi-
fying the people or things each of which did, or does, or has done, or will do,
something in a manner the degree of which is being compared.

In the examples below, for manner, the present-indefinite tense is used, since
the actions expressed are more or less of the permanent nature:

(a) Adjectives

Muka Rwakaikara naakiza muka Balinda obukooto.
Rwakaikara’s wife is bigger than Balinda’s wife.
(Lit. Rwakaikara’s exceeds Balinda’s wife in bigness.)

Entale neekiza embogo obuke.
A lion is smaller than a buffalo.
(Lit. A lion exceeds a buffalo in smallness.)

Here the questions are introduced by the pronominal root -ha? ‘who?
which?’, followed by the verb okukira in its causative form and the prono-
minal root -ndi ‘other’, or the variable phrase mugenzi we ‘his/her partner’.
Answers to such questions will also contain a self-standing pronoun to which
the copula ni- has been added if the subject and the predicate are not
represented by only the noun, e.g.

Nooha arukukiza ondi/mugenzi we, obukooto, muka Balinda, nandiki/
rundi, muka Rwakaikara?

Who exceeds the other in bigness, Balinda’s or Rwakaikara’s wife?

Muka Rwakaikara.

Rwakaikara’s wife does.

The same thing applies to questions:

(Lit. It is Rwakaikara’s that surpasses Balinda’s wife in bigness.)

Kiki ekirukukira ekindi obuke, embogo, nandiki/rundi, entale?
Which is smaller, a buffalo or a lion?

Entale.

Entale niyo erukukira embogo obuke.

A lion is smaller than a buffalo.

(Lit. It is the lion that surpasses a buffalo in smallness.)

(b) Adverbs

Bagonza naakira Balinda kubaza kurungi.
Bagonza speaks more clearly than Balinda.
(Lit. Bagonza exceeds Balinda in clear speech.)

Motoka neekira egaali y’omwika kwiruka
A car travels faster than a train.
(Lit. A car exceeds the train in running fast.)

Again questions here follow the same pattern as that followed in (b) and (a)
above, e.g.

Nooha akira ondi/mugenzi we, okubaza kurungi, Balinda nandiki/ rundi
Bagonza?

C. Comparison of adjectives and adverbs 317

Muka Balinda mukooto.

Muka Rwakaikara mukooto okumukira.
Muka Baguma mukooto okubakira bombi.
Balinda’s wife is big.

Rwakaikara’s wife is bigger.

Baguma’s wife is the biggest of all.

(ii) Constructions with the verb okukira in an appropriate tense followed by
the numeral -ombi (both) with an abstract noun

Entale nkooto.

Embogo nkooto okugikira.

Enjojo neekira byombi obukooto.
A lion is big.

A buffalo is bigger than a lion.

An elephant is the biggest of the two.

(iii) Constructions with the prepositional verb okukiira (surpass beyond
measure) followed by the adverb kimu (utterly, completely), the numeral
-ombi (both) and an abstract noun

Omwana wa Bagonza mukuru.

Owa Kunanuka mukuru okumukira.

Owa Bagambaki naakiirra kimu bombi obukuru.
Bagonza’s child is old.

Kunanuka’s child is older than his.

Bagambaki’s child is the oldest of all.

(iv) Constructions with the verb okukira in its causative form followed by
the numeral -ombi (both) and an abstract noun derived from the adjective
being compared

mwana wa Bagonza mukuru.
Owa Kunanuka mukuru okumukira.
Owa Bagambaki naakiza bombi obukuru.
Bagonza’s child is old.
Kunanuka’s child is older.
Bagambaki’s child is the oldest of all.

When a person or thing is singled out of a group, a self-standing pronoun to
which the copula ni- has been added as well as the numeral root -ona to
which an appropriate numeral concord has been added are used as shown
below:

(iv) Constructions with the verb okukira in its causative form followed by
the numeral -ombi (both) and an action verb in the infinitive put at the end
of the sentence

Abasaija barra.

Abakazi kakiza abasaija kurra.
Abaana bakiza bombi, abasaija
Men weep much.

Women weep more than men.
Children weep most of all.

n’abakazi kurra.

Here again when a person or thing is singled out of a group, a self-standing
pronoun to which the copula ni- has been added is used. It is also followed

y the verb okukira in an appropriate tense, the name of the people or things
surpassed, the numeral root -ona to which an appropriate numeral concord
has been added then the verb expressing the action in the infinitive form
together with its qualifier at the end of the sentence, e.g.

Ekitindinda nikyo kikira ebyekuurra ebindi byona kugenda mpora.
It is the snail which travels most slowly of all creeping creatures.

(v) Comparison of adverbs

Some adverbs which qualify verbs in terms of time and place may also be
compared. Examples:

1. Rwakaikara aizire kara.
Bamuhiiga aizire kara muno okumukira.
Babwemara aizire kara muno/okukira/okubakira/okukiirra kimu
bombi (Rwakaikara na bamuhiiga).
Rwakaikara came early.
Bamuhiiga came earlier.
Bwabwemara came earliest of all.

2. Kuruga Kaseese kuhika Kampala, hara.
Kuruga Kaseese kuhika Nairobi hara muno okukira kuruga
Kaseese kuhika Kampala.
Kuruga Kaseese kuhika Mombasa hara muno okukiirra kimu
byombi (kuruga Kaseese kuhika Kampala n’okuruga Kaseese
kuhika Mombasa).
Kampala is far from Kaseese.
Nairobi is farther from Kasese than Kampala.
Mombasa is farthest of all from Kaseese.

CHAPTER SEVENTEEN

SENTENCES IN WHICH JOINING WORDS OR
PARTICLES ARE USED

Sentences in which Joining Words or Particles are used are given here so as
to enable Runyoro-Rutooro students to be familiar with the way joining
words or particles are translated in English. Because of the prepositions or
conjunctions which translate them in English, some Bantu grammarians have
referred to joining words or particles as prepositions or conjunctions accord-
ing to the words used in English. But it will be seen in the examples below
that many of the joining words are words or a combination of words which
normally do a different grammatical function.

A. GENITIVE PARTICLES OR WORDS WHICH EXPRESS POSSES-
SION

wa: as in Omwana wa Byaruhanga ‘Byaruhanga’s child’
ba: " " Abaana ba Byaruhanga ‘Byaruhanga’s children’
gwa:" " Omutwe gwa Kugonza ‘Kugonza’s head’

ya: " " Emitaano ya Uganda ‘Uganda’s boundaries’

lya: " "  Eryato ly’abasohi ‘The fishermen’s boat’

kya: " " Ekigambo kya Ruhanga ‘God’s word’

ga: " " Amagezi g’abantu ‘People’s wisdom’

bya: " " Ebitabu by’abeegi ‘the pupils’ books’

za: " " Embuzi za Byakutaaga ‘Byakutaaga’s goats’
rwa:" " Orugoye rwa mutabani we ‘his son’s cloth’

ka: " "  Akasozi ka nseeri hali ‘yonder hill’

twa: " " Otwizi twa Rulendera ‘Rulendera’s little water’
bwa:" " Obwana bwa Ruyonga ‘Ruyonga’s little children’
kwa:" " Okweta kwa Ruhanga ‘God’s call’

Note the elision of the -a of relationship when followed by a word beginning
with a vowel.

C. CO-ORDINATING PARTICLES

1. Joining two words of the same grammatical function

na: Rwabudongo na ireeta ekyonzira batamba kimu.
Rwabudongo and Ireeta offer the same sacrifice.
hamu na: Embuzi hamu n’ente bisoro by’omumaka.
Goats and cows are domestic animals.

rundi: Mpa enyama rundi encu.
Give me either meat or fish.

kandi: Kateesigwa mugara kandi murofu.
Kateesigwa is both lazy and dirty.

CONDITIONS EXPRESSED BY VERBS WITHOUT A CONDI: —

TIONAL TENSE PREFIX

s has already been said many conditions in Runyoro-Rutooro may be ©
enracead hy verhe without conditional tense pnrefixes. Those conditions

F, Conditions expressed by verbs 273

which make use of the time tenses of the indicative mood may be made with
verbs in simple or compound tenses.

In compound tenses the auxiliary verb okuba expresses the required tense,
The stem of the main verb, that is the verb which in the simple form would
have expressed the requried tense, must always be modified. In sentences
which make suppositions the verb in the apodosis is normally in the present-
imperfect tense. The rest will keep the tense expressed by the verb in the
protasis.

The following are the ways in which conditions expressed by verbs without
a conditional tense prefix both in simple and compound tenses may be made:

1, PRESENT INDEFINITE
(1) With obu

By the use of verbs in the present-indefinite tense both in the protasis and
apodosis with obu as an introductory word. Sentences made in this way
merely state the condition on which an action is normally done, e.g.

(a) Simple forms
(i) Positive

Obu omuha omulimo agukora.
If you give him some work he will do it.

Obu omubaliza mpora ayetegereza.
If you speak to him slowly he will understand.

(ii) Negative

Obu otamuha mulimo takora kantu.
If you do not give him some work he will not do anything.

Obu otamubaliza mpora tayetegereza.
If you do not speak to him slowly he will not understand.

(b) Compound forms
(i) Positive

Obu oba omuhaire omulimo agukora.
If you care to give him some work he will do it.

Obu oba amubaliize mpora ayetegereza.
If you care to speak to him slowly he will understand.

if you do not car to speak to him slowly he will not understand.

2. PRESENT IMPERFECT

(1) With kakuba

By the use of verbs in the present imperfect tense both in the protasis and
apodosis with kakuba as an introductory word. Sentences merely make a
supposition, e.g.

(a) Simple forms
(i) Positive

Kakuba noomuha omulimo naagukora.
If you gave him some work he would do it.

(ii) Negative

Kuba otarukumuha mulimo tarukukora.
f you did not give him some work he would be idle.

(b) Compound forms
(1) Positive

Kuba nooba omuhaire omulimo naagukora.
If you gave him some work he would do it.

(ii) Negative

Kuba nooba otamuhaire mulimo tarukukora.
If you did not give him some work he would be idle.

(2) Without an introductory word

In ordinary speech the introductory word kuba and the verb okuba coming
next to it in the present-imperfect may be omitted and the following forms
used:

(i) Positive

Omuhaire omulimo naagukora.
If you gave him some work he would do it.

(ii) Negative

Otamuhaire mulimo tarukukora.
If you did not give him some work he would be idle.

Obu oraaba wali omuhaire omulimo akagukora.
If you had (before a certain time in the far-past) given him some work
presumably he did it.

Obu oraaba otamuhaire(ge) mulimo takozire.
If you did not give him some work presumably he did not work.

Obu oraaba wali otamuhaire mulimo atakole.
If you did not (before a certain time in the far-past) give him some work
presumably he did not work.

Obu oraaba obaire(ge) otamuhaire mulimo takozire.
If you had not (at that time) given him some work presumably he did
not work.

from me.

4. FAR FUTURE

By the use of the verb in the far-future both in the protasis and the apodosis,
with obu as an introductory word. These sentences indicate what may
happen in future if a condition is fulfilled, e.g.

(a) Simple forms

(i) Positive

Obu olimuha omulimo aligukora.
If you give him some work (in the far-future) he will do it.

(ii) Negative

Obu otalimuha mulimo talikora.
If (in the far future) you do not give him some work he will be idle.

6. RELATIVE CONDITIONAL PHRASES

(1) Introduction

These have not been discussed alongside direct conditional phrases as has
been done for phrases in the indicative mood, because their use is so
complex that in order to understand it a special study of it is necessary.

Relative conditional phrases are used whenever it becomes necessary to
emphasize not only the person or thing which would have done something if
a condition were fulfilled, but also the person or thing the action of which is,
or would have been, the condition for one to do something.

The conditional tense prefix for them is -aaku-; but as it is used throughout
all the tenses, it may not help one to tell the tense in which a relative condi-
tional phrase is unless one examines the tense expressed by the clause before
it.

Relative conditional phrases which put emphasis on the person or thing
which would have done something if a condition were fulfilled occur in the
apodosis of a conditional sentence introduced by kakuba or obu.

Relative conditional phrases which put emphasis on the person or thing the
action of which is, or would have been, the condition for one to do some-
thing occur in the protasis of a conditional sentence and may be introduced


--- page9 ---
Chapter fifteen: Compound tenses

uses beginning with words other than kakuba or obu. Very often such
+3 contain the verb -li whether or not a compound tense is in sue. The
expressing what might be done if a condition were fulfilled, in the
ysis, also takes the conditional tense prefix -aaku-.

ive conditional phrases which occur in the apodosis have the normal
enses of the conditional mood, namely, the present-perfect (including
ear-past) and the far-past. Relative conditional phrases which occur in
rotasis have three tenses, namely, the present, the present-perfect and
ir-past tenses.

erbs expressing relative conditional phrases modify their stems. The
exception is the verb which expresses the present tense of the relative
itional phrases.

iples of how relative conditional phrases are used are given below. Note
e present-perfect tense of relative conditional phrases used in condi-
| sentences introduced by kakuba or obu the use of the copula ni
sdiately after a relative conditional phrase and of the self-standing
oun to which the copula ni has been added, when the person or thing
iasized comes before a relative conditional phrase. Note also that in the
ast tense of this category of relative conditional phrases the copula ni is
ced by the verb okuba having -ku- as its conditional tense prefix. This
x must be used by any verb in a direct conditional phrase used after a
ive conditional phrase in conditional sentences with relative conditional
se and having kakuba or obu as their introductory word:

(Lit. the person who would welcome him home would be

Kanyunyuuzi).

ayaakumutangiriire.

If he came Kanyunyuuzi would be the only person to welcome him
home. (Lit. Kanyunyuuzi would be the only person who would welcome
him home).

7, CONDITIONAL TENSE PREFIXES IN NON-CONDITIONAL PHRASES

There are many words in Runyoro-Rutooro which may be used with
conditional tense prefixes -ku-, -aku- and -aaku- without expressing a
conditional idea, which must be distinguished from conditional phrases.
They are used in sentences such as the following:

Ngambira nkooku yaakwizire ataina motoka y’okumuleeta?
Tell me how he would have come without the means of a car?

Haakire waakumufubiire omukiikaro ky’okumubinga.

It would have been better if you punished him instead of expelling him.
Nkooku babaire bataina mirimo nyingi yaakubalekere bagenda
basoma.

As they did not have a lot of work he should have let them go to church.

NTENCES WITH -NYAKU-

particle is used in the present-indefinite and the present-perfect includ-
he near-past tenses. In addition to distinguishing a positive relative
e with the vowel a or e as its relative concord from a positive direct
e using the same vowel as its pronominal concord, when used with
relative concords it also denotes something which is already known to
the speaker and the person spoken to, or to the majority of people. It
s immediately after a relative concord and is used only in positive
ve phrases. Its function may be better understood by comparing the
wing examples:

elative phrases without the particle -nyaku-

elative phrases with the particle -nyaku-
ith ordinary verbs

a

G. The particles -nyaku-, owa- and eya- 291

(b) With -ina and -li

The particle -nyaku- is also used in relative phrases built up on the verbs
-ina- and -li- for the same purpose. Compare thus:

(i) The verb -ina

Aina ekitabu kyange nooha?

Who has my book?

(Someone has my book but I do not know who. I now want to know
him.)

Anyakwina ekitabu kyange ogu nooha?
Who is that man with my book?
(Someone has my book. I see him but I do not know who he is.)

(ii) The verb -li

Mwana ki ali aho?
Which child is there?
(I hear the noise as of a child but I am not quite some of that.)

Mwana ki anyakuli aho?

Which child is there?

(I am sure that is a child somewhere but I want to know which one it is
still.)

4, SENTENCES WITH OWA-= AND EYA-

(1) Introduction

In order to distinguish relative phrases with the vowel a or e as their subject
relative concord from direct phrases using the same vowel as their subject
pronominal concord, in the near and the far-future tenses the particles owa-
and eya- may be attached to normal subject relative concords. Owa- may be
attached to a relative concord represented by the vowel a, while eya- may be
attached to a relative concord represented by the vowel e.

It is important to note that these particles become part of a relative phrase,
because the concords to which they get attached are subject relative con-
cords. Generally they are never part of any word when functioning as geni-
tive particles or as object relatives (see pp. 62f).

These particles may be used both in positive and negative forms of relative
phrases despite the fact that in negative forms no difficulty of distinguishing
a direct from a relative phrase exists.

eya: Nte ki eyeetakamirwe(ge)?

Which cow was not milked?

| CHAPTER SIXTEEN

ADJECTIVES AND ADVERBS

j A. ADJECTIVES

Since there are few roots which are basically adjectives, the word ‘adjective’
in this chapter is therefore used to cover all the words which qualify the
noun in Runyoro-Rutooto. Such words may be:

— True adjectives
— Nouns alone
— Possessive nouns
— Relative phrases in the present-imperfect tense
— Relative phrases in the present-perfect tense
— Pronouns
— Numerals

1. TRUE ADJECTIVES

Examples of these are given on p. 34. They describe the noun in terms of
| # quality. The following are some of the roots for such adjectives. Note that
; . some of them were given on p. 43.

-bi ‘bad, evil, dangerous’ -ceke ‘weak, feeble’
-gazi ‘wide, broad’ -hango ‘tall and big’
-hyaka ‘new’ -ke ‘small, little, few’
-kome ‘chosen’ -kooto ‘big, large’
-kuru ‘old, mature, adult, great, -raira ‘tall’
important’
-rungi ‘good, nice, fine’ -sigazi ‘young male’
beautiful, pretty’
-taito ‘small, few’ -tiitiiro ‘very small, wee’
-to ‘small, not fully grown’ -bisi ‘raw, uncooked, unripe, fresh’
-rurumi ‘fierce’

JOUNS AS QUALIFIERS OF OTHER NOUNS

ny nouns in Runyoro-Rutooro may function as qualifiers of other nouns
; making it difficult for one to distinguish a real adjective from a noun.
mine the following examples:

Omukazi omurungi
The good woman

Omukazi omulimi
A woman capable of digging

he first example omurungi is a real adjective, but in the second example
ulimi is a noun used as qualifier of another noun. There are some other
mples of such nouns. Note that they also describe the noun in terms of
lity.

Omusaija omutiini

Fearful man

Omwana omuhoole
An insolent child

Omukazi omugara
Lazy woman

Omusaija omukozi
Industrious man

Ihanga eriguuda
A rich country

B. Adverbs

(d) Formed with ka-

This is attached to numeral roots as well as adjectival roots, e.g.

Drink a little (from -ke, ‘small’).

(e) Formed with ma-
This, too, is attached to noun stems, e.g.

Rwana masaija.
Fight like a man (from omusaija, ‘man’).

Naagenda mate.
He is walking like a cow (from ente, ‘cow’).

Atiina makazi.

He fears like a woman (from omukazi, ‘woman’).

Ayezingire mabwa.

He has rolled up himself like a dog (from embwa, ‘dog’).

8 Chapter twelve: Derivative verbs in particular

) Place

at is the place where the action was or has been or will be completed. In
e case of motion verbs the place where motion is directed, e.g.

Omukazi tabohera bintu mukisiika.
A woman does not pack up things in the inner sitting room.

Enyama bagitematemera hakiti.
People cut meat into pieces on a piece of wood.

Bakairukira omukibira.
They took refuge in the forest.

}) Time
hat is the time one takes to have something done or the time by which one
oes something, e.g.
Oruhanda orw’okuruga Kyaka okuhika Butiiti twarulibatiraga
ebiro bibiri.
It took us two days to travel from Kyaka to Butiiti.

Omusiri gunu akagulimira okwezi kumu.
It took her one month to cultivate this garden.

Ha yaiziire uwe akaba agenzire ira.
By the time he arrived he had already gone.

4) Cause, reason, purpose or motive

Bakwetera kulya ngu “olikankomaho?”
Why do you have to complain when someone has called you to eat?

(Lit. Do you have to say “you will one day put me in trouble” simply —

because someone has called you for eating?.)

Omwana omuteeriire ki?
For what reason have you had to beat the child?

Nimukunganira ki?
What is the cause for your quarrel?

Agenderayo kulya.
His aim of going there is to eat.

Omwana mukaise tamwonkera burungi.
It is not for pleasure that a child has to suck its step mother.

A. Prepositional 189

(5) Completeness or thoroughness of the action

Agendiire kimu.
He has gone for good.

Waakubaire olekiire kimu n’okwija.
It would have been better if you did not come at all.

Izira kimu n’ebyawe.
Come with all your belongings.

2. NEW MEANINGS

(1) Apart from the above uses, a prepositional verb suffix in many cases
imparts a new meaning to the stem of the simple verb to which it is added

e.g.

Original Verb Prepositional Verb New Meaning Imparted to

Original Verb

okuboha ‘to tie’ okubohera ‘to tie cow’s

legs with strip of hide when

milking’

okutemera ‘to cut for’ okutemera ‘to plant beans
one by one with a hoe’

okubohera ‘to tie for’

okutema ‘to cut’

(2) A new meaning may also be imparted to a prepositional verb itself if,
when its action is directed to a person or thing, time, cause, reason, purpose
or motive, an attempt is made to direct its action further so as to implicate
place.

The prepositional verb to which a new meaning has been imparted is
generally referred to as a double prepositional verb or propositional verb 2
(V.Pr. 2).

To avoid confusion brought about by the new meaning introduced by
prepositional verb 2 the original prepositional verb extends its stem further
with the result that both, itself and prepositional verb 2, share the same
extension, e.g.

okubohera ‘to tie for’

okuboheerra (V. Pr. 2,; ‘to fasten up for’ (as food for one going on a
journey)).

okubohereerra ‘to tie for or fasten up for’ (with reference to place in
respect to okubohera and okuboheerra)

okutemeerra ‘to cut grass with a knife
okutemereerra ‘to cut grass for or on behalf of one’ (with reference to

following sentences may be of some help in the understanding of how
ositional verbs extend their stems for various notions:

Omwojo akatema omuti.
The boy felled a tree.

He felled it for an old man.

Akagumutemereerra omumusire gwe (not akagumutemeerra)
He felled it for him from his garden.

pare with the following:

Omusaija akatemeerra ebitooke.

The man cleared grass from the banana plantation.

Yabitemerereerra Buganda.
He cleared the grass while he was in Buganda.

rent words.

JRMATION

suffix for the applied forms of many verbs is -ira or -era. Exceptions to
rule are discussed hereunder:

/erbs ending in -ra

s in -ra make their prepositional forms by prefixing an r to the last
ble thus causing length to the preceding vowel, e.g.

okusara ‘to cut’
okukora ‘to work’

okusaarra ‘to cut for, to be bitter’
okukoorra ‘to work within a certain
length of time’

A. Prepositional 191

(2) Verbs ending in -rra

Verbs in -rra restore the dropped vowel and then prefix -r to the last
syllable. There is again a long vowel sound before the last syllable, e.g.

okukoorra ‘to cough’ okukoroorra ‘to cough at’
okuseerra ‘to look for’ okuseruurra ‘to look for something
somewhere’

(3) Verbs ending in -ja

Verbs in -ja which are preceded by i follow the general rule but undergo
sound change (see pp. 26f and exceptions thereof).

(4) Verbs ending in -za

Verbs in -za (many of them causative verbs) change -za into -liza or -leza as
sound change allows, e.g.

okuguza ‘to sell’
okubaza ‘to speak’

okuguliza ‘to sell in or at a place’
okubaliza ‘to speak, to.speak while at...’

(5) Causative verbs ending in -ya
Causatives in -ya change ya into -iriza or -ereza as sound change allows,
e.g.

okubyamya ‘to make lie’ okubyamiriza ‘to lay one on
something’

okudomya ‘to make stupid’ okudomereza ‘to make one
stupid while one is somewhere’

(6) Causative verbs ending in -sa

Causatives in -sa replace the final a by -iza or -eza as sound change allows,
e.g.
okuliisa ‘to graze’
okusomesa ‘to teach’

okuliisiza ‘to rare for or at’
okusomeseza ‘to teach for’

(7) Passive verbs ending in -wa

Passive forms ending in -wa replace -wa by -irwa or erwa as sound change
allows, e.g.


--- page3 ---
Chapter twelve: Derivative verbs in particular

okutoijerwa ‘to be tilled ....’

okutoijwa ‘to be tilled’
okutonderwa ‘to be identified .”

okutondwa ‘to be identified’

Passive verbs ending in -ibwa or -ebwa

sives in -ibwa or -ebwa replace -ibwa and -ebwa by -irizibwa or
.zebwa as sound change allows e.g.

okubyamirizibwa ‘to be laid ...’

okugonderezebwa ‘to be loved...’
okubalirizibwa ‘to be talked ...’

okubyamibwa ‘to be laid down’

okugonzebwa ‘to be loved’

okubazibwa ‘to be spoken by’
ce these latter forms are rarely used here are some examples to illustrate
ir use.

Omwana enkeeto akagibyamizibwa nyina.
The child was laid on the skin by its mother.

Omuurro gukagondezebwa ki kandi kunu gwokya ebintu?
Why should fire be loved when in fact it burns things?

Eki kikabalizibwa obutamanya.
That was said through ignorance.

C. CAUSATIVE

|. FUNCTION

These are verb forms which indicate that the action of the radical verb has
been put in motion as a result of a person or thing acting as an agent on its
own or through another agent. Their formation is discussed as follows:

2. FUNCTION

(1) Simple monosyllabic verb stems

Simple monosyllabic verb stems make their causative by adding -isa to their
simple stems, e.g.

4. Chapter twelve: Derivative verbs in particular

ba ‘be’ baisa ‘put in a state
ha ‘give’ haisa ‘cause one to give’

ta ‘put, leave’ taisa®® ‘cause one to put or leave’

ut note the exception in sa ‘grind’, siisa ‘grind with’, fa ‘die’, fiisa ‘be
ereaved of, especially children’.

) Palatalised and labialised monosyllabic verb stems

Aonosyllabic verb stems consisting of “y- and w-compounds” change the
nal a to -isa, but in some words the original vowel which in the simple
orm is replaced by another sound is here restored, e.g.

ewisa ‘help one to break, allow to escape’
hoisa ‘cause to get finished, go dry (cow)
hiisa ‘finish cooking, brew’

cwa ‘break’
hwa ‘get finished’
hya ‘get cooked’

3) Simple verb stems ending in -ra or -rra

Jerbs in ra (but not prepositional) including verbs in rra (prepositional or
on-prepositional) change -ra into -za”, e.g.

okubara ‘to count’ okubaza ‘to help one count’
okuhaarra ‘to scrape together’ | okuharuza ‘to scrape out with’
okuhaarra ‘to be worried’ okuharaza ‘to worry a person’

Note the restoration of the dropped vowel between the two r’s Mt
ykuharuza, okuharaza and the loss of the long vowel sound in the verb
oot.

probably due to unrealised influence of causative forms for sa and fa (see “Bul

taito ‘little’ as tiito. |

Prepositional verbs in ra, which originate from associative or reciprocal
verbs replace the final a by -iza, e.g.

(angaanira from tangaana ‘meat’ becomes tangaaniriza, gonzanganira
from gonzangana ‘love one another’ becomes gonzanganiriza, etc.

(d) Important notes

(1) Philologically one could say that originally all prepositional verbs in ra
teplaced their last syllable by za to express a causative meaning. But in the
vourse of time the suffix was either dropped or retained by some verbs so as
\o help them introduce words with new meaning, e.g.

okubohera ‘to tie a cow's legs with a strip of hide when milking’
okuboheza ‘to make use of the strip of hide or any other material in
tying a cow's legs when milking’

okutemera ‘to plant beans one by one with a hoe’

okutemeza ‘to make use of a hoe or any other tool in planting beans
one bv one’ etc.

8) All the other intransitive verbs

‘he rest of the intransitive verbs replace the final a by -ya, e.g.

C. Causative 197

okuhaba ‘to go astray, lose one's way’ okuhabya ‘to make one lose

Notes:

(a) The verb stem to which the suffix -ya is added acquires a transitive
meaning and its action is put into motion by a primary agent (person or
thing).

(b) Because of the transitive meaning which causative verbs in -ya get, their
stems may, therefore, take both a passive and a causative-passive form and
also be further extended by means of the suffix -isa or -esa so as to give
them the same value as transitive verbs get when the suffix -isa or -esa is
added to them. But when this is done, the new form is referred to as double
causative verb, or verb causative 2.

(9) Transitive verbs

Transitive verbs (including causative verbs in -ya to which the suffix -isa or
-esa is added indicate that a person or thing has)

(a) acted as a primary agent or an agent working through a secondary agent,
or

(b) helped with the fulfilment of the action of the radical verb, that has been
put in motion (here the form expresses “‘participation’”’) or

(c) forced or persuaded one to do something.

verb 1, are reffered to as causative verb 2. Causatives in -isa or -esa which
do not originate from causative verb, when they are further extended by the

me suffix to indicate a secondary agent are also referred to as causative
verb 2.

10) Important notes on the use of the suffixes -ya, -isa and -esa
a) Some Runyoro-Rutooro speakers will be heard using -iisa, e.g.

nagiisa _ instead of nagisisa ‘to help to throw away’ q
jumiisa instead of jumisisa ‘to make a person do or say things which
will be interpreted as an insult to another person, cause one to be
blamed’ I
gesiisa _ instead of gesesisa ‘help one to reap’

This sound is just a distortion of -isa after it has been added to -isa or esa”,
b) The suffix -ya is not added to some intransitive verbs if the verbs thus

ormed may not have any practical meaning. Instead of -ya such verbs take
isa or -esa as sound change allows, e.g.

okuruga ‘to come from, get out of way’
okurugisa ‘to make one get out of way’ and not okurugya, e.g.

Ekyakurugisa ha oikaliire kiki?
Why have you had to leave your seat?
(Lit. What has caused you to leave where you have been sitting?)

fo put the action of the radical verb in motion, e.g.

okutembya ‘to cause to ascend, raise, hoist’
okuhamba ‘to take by force, carry off, ravish’
okuhambya ‘to chase, run after’

(ii) to impart a new meaning to the radical verb, e.g.

okuraha ‘to scratch like a hen, rake ‘fire’

okurahya ‘to turn over green grass (as in search of grasshoppers)’
okusoma ‘to take a sip of, peck’

okusomya ‘to eat potatoes, etc. with liquid, e.g. churned milk’

(11) Causatives from the rest of the derivative verbs

Formation of causatives from the rest of the derivative verbs (no causatives
are formed from neuter verbs) follows the rules governing the syllables in
which they end, e.g.

okubambura ‘to unpeg’ okubambuza
okwesereka ‘to hide oneself? okweserekesa
okutematema ‘to cut at close intervals’ okutematemesa

Ekikubambwize oruhu runu rutakoomere kiki?
What caused you to unpeg this skin before it got dry?

Eki kyonka kikweserekesa abantu?
Can a simple matter like that make you hide yourself from people?

Leka kutematemesa ekipanga kye omwitaka.
Don’t cut earth at close intervals with his big knife.

D. NEUTER
|, FUNCTION

Certain verbs which indicate the possibility or capability of the action of the
simple verb to be put in motion or which tell what the state of something
fter a simple verb has been put in motion is are generally termed neuter” by
some Bantu grammarians. But in Runyoro - Rutooro these notions may also
be expressed by verbs in passive forms.

Some Bantu grammarians call these verbs Stative or Verbs of State. But it is
believed that neither of the term Neuter or Stative covers all the implications they

--- page7 ---
)0 Chapter twelve: Derivative verbs in particular

FORMATION

euter verbs are formed in the following ways:

|.) By replacing the final vowel of the simple verb stem by the suffixes -ika,
‘ka, -ooka and -uuka according to how sound change allows, e.g.

okwatika ‘to be smashed’
okucweka ‘to be broken’
okugoorrooka ‘to be stretched out’

okwata ‘to smash’
okuewa ‘to break’
okugoorra ‘to stretch out,
straighten, iron’

okuhuuha ‘to blow’ okuhuuhuuka ‘to be blown off

2) By replacing the last syllable of some verbs ending in -ra by -ka, e.g.

okusoboka ‘to be manageable’
okutaaguka ‘to get torn’

okujumbuka ‘to dash out, rush out :
(of a hole, etc.)

okusobora ‘to manage’
okutaagura ‘to tear in pieces’
okujumbura ‘to start prey
from its lair’

Jote that some of the verbs in -ra and -ka in (2) above are actually
onversive verbs in -ura and -uka respectively (see pp. 201f). Because of
ack of practicability of their meaning simple stems of some neuter verbs are
ot easily determined, e.g.

okutweka ‘to put load on head of? okuheeka ‘to carry a child on the
back’

okutandika ‘to begin’ okusomeka ‘to insert, to clip in’

Note that many of these are transitive verbs denoting actions of putting
something into position.

3) Expression by passive forms ij

The following are the examples in which possibility or capability of action
of the simple verb may be denoted by a verb in the passive form:

okusomwa ‘to be read’
okwegesebwa ‘to be taught’
okutambwa ‘to be cured’

okusoma ‘to read’
okwegesa ‘to teach’
okutamba ‘to cure’

Kinu ekigambo tikirukusomwa.
This word is unreadable.

Omuntu w’empaka tayegesebwa.
An argumentative person is never taught.

D. Neuter 201

Bunu oburwaire nibutambwa Abarukwiragura.
This disease is cured only by Black Africans.

E. CONVERSIVE
1. FUNCTION

These verbs are sometimes termed Reversive by some Bantu grammarians.
But since this term applies only to those verbs which indicate the opposite of
what is expressed by the verb root, e.g. okubamba ‘to peg out’ and
okubambura ‘to unpeg’ the term Conversive is adopted so as to enable us to
include even verbs which go far beyond the term Reversive in the list. The
term Conversive is taken to mean a change from the meaning of the verb
root’.

2. FORMATION

The verbs are formed by replacing the final vowel or syllable of the simple
verb stem by -ura, -uurra, -uka, -uuka, -uruka, -urruuka, -ora, -oorra,
-oka, -oroka and -orrooka according to how sound change allows, e.g.

okubamba ‘to peg out, fix hide on a drum’
okubambura ‘to unpeg, pull up’
okubambuurra ‘to unfix, e.g. hide from a drum’
okubambuka ‘to come unpegged’
okubamburuka ‘to come unfixed’
okubamburruuka ‘to come unpegged’

okusoma ‘to read, take a sip, come into prominence, e.g. flame or fire,
extend into the land, e.g. sea or lake water’

okusomora ‘to draw out or off, e.g. sword, take out one by one, undo
stitches’

okusomoorra ‘to bear fruit, e.g. beans, to take out continually in big
quantity’

okusomoka ‘to come out of and fall’

okusomoroka ‘to go away stealthly one by one’

okusomorrooka ‘to be rendered small as a result of part of the whole
having been continually taken out’ etc.

AB)

See A Luganda Grammar by Ashton, Ndawula and Tucker. p. 352.

(3) Notes

(a) There is no guiding principle as to when to apply -ngama or -na. The use
of each of these suffixes is learnt as one masters the language. If carelessly
used the suffixes may give rise to some forms which are socially
unacceptable.

(b) There are many other verbs in -na conveying a greater or lesser
implication of association, which apparently do not have active stems from
which they originate. If at all they do, such stems have been for so long out
of use that their existence is now quite insignificant. Their examples are:

okutangaana ‘to meet’
okufukaana ‘to wrestle’

okuteerana ‘to co-operate’
okuzigaana ‘to play a game with
string (cat’s cradle)’

okusindaana ‘to compete with’ okwahukana ‘to differ, part from’

(c) Even words with no clear illustration of association may also be included
here, e.g.

okutalibana ‘to work alone and very hard, to do all work oneself’
okuhakana ‘to deny, dispute, argue, compete’

G. REFLEXIVE

Basically these are transitive verbs which take prefix e- for all persons,
singular and plural, and where the sense allows, for all classes, e.g.

Active Form
okufoora ‘to make to become, change’
okusereka ‘to hide’

Reflexive Form
okwefoora ‘to change oneself?
kwesereka ‘to hide oneself’

There are some verbs with an apparent prefix e-. These must not be confused
with Reflexive verbs. They could either be transitive or intransitive verbs,
ep.

okwekunika ‘to be proud, be stuck up, give oneself airs’

okwehooga ‘to repudiate an acquaintance, deny knowledge of? etc.

-CONCLUDING REMARKS ON VERBAL DERIVATION

.STATIC AND CONDUCTIVE VERBS

“here are some forms of derivative verbs discussed by some Bantu |
rammarians in other languages which have not been discussed in this
anguage. They include forms such as “static” verbs in -ma or “Conductive”
rerbs in -ta. The reason why they have not been discussed as such is that
vhile they are generally accepted as derivative verbs in some other Bantu
anguages their formation in Runyoro-Rutooro is so divorced from the
‘ormation of other derivative verbs that few or no students or scholars of this

anguage have recognised them as such.

CHAPTER THIRTEEN

MOODS AND TENSES

Mention has already ben made about what moods are and what each one of
them does in a sentence. What remains now is to discuss them in terms of
formation of their tenses.

Runyoro-Rutooro tenses may not conveniently be translated into European
languages such as English, because of the differences that exist between a
European and an African language not only in number but also in the
meaning expressed by each tense in both languages. In Runyoro-Rutooro
each mood (except one) has its own tenses:

A. THE INFINITIVE MOOD

This mood expresses no tense. But see note (c) on p. 212. It is formed by
adding the fifteenth noun class prefix to the simple stem which ends in -a as
examples on p. 178 show. The negative form may, however, be substituted
by the fourteenth noun class prefix to express a verbal noun in abstract, e.g.

okutahweza ‘not to see clearly’ obutahweza ‘blindness’
okutakora ‘not to work’ obutakora ‘idleness’
okuteegonza ‘not to feel happy’ §obuteegonza ‘unhappiness’

B. THE IMPERATIVE MOOD

This is now discussed under the following tenses:

|, PRESENT

(1) Function

This tense expresses a simple command that must be obeyed at the time of
speaking.

(2) Formation

lor its positive form in the singular the command may consist of:

(f) The v

In both singular and plural forms the negative may also be expressed by the
stem of the auxiliary verb okuleka ‘to cease, stop, leave of doing’. The
action to be stopped is expressed by the inifinitive which must come
immediately after the negative expressed by the stem of the verb okuleka,
eg.

Teera engoma, leka kuteera nyowe.
Beat the drum not me!

(Lit. Beat the drum, stop beating me!)

Muleke kwesiga ebitaina mugaso.
Do not trust things which have no use!

Important note:

What applies to the Present Tense applies equally to the Continuous Present
(see below) when the command is expressed by a transitive verb and it is
necessary to involve either the first person plural or the third person, both
singular and plural, acting as the object of the command.

2. CONTINUOUS PRESENT

(1) Function

This tense (apparently overlooked in the past) expresses a command to be
obeyed for a time, on several occasions, or habitually, or denotes an action to
be more or less prolonged.

(2) Formation

The singular takes the suffix -ga but the plural is in the Subjunctive with the
suffix -ge. In other words the plural takes a form similar to that of the
Continuous-future (see pp. 214f). The two tenses, however, differ not only in
their meaning and the time each one of them expresses but also in the way
they form their negative. Examples:

(3) Notes

(a) The suffix -ge- becomes -ga- in the plural form of the verb stem to which
the enclitic -ho- has been added. Compare this:

Murugege ha buli muntu weena arukugonza kubahabya.
Murugehoga buli muntu weena arukugonza kubahabya.
Depart from any person who intends to mislead you.

(b) As is the case with the present, the negative form of this tense is
sometimes expressed by the stem of the auxiliary verb okuleka ‘to cease,
stop, leave off doing’. In the singular it is used with the suffix -ga- and in the
plural it is used subjunctively with the suffix -ge-. The main verb at which
negation is directed must be in the infinitive, e.g.

Negative (singular)

Lekagaga kwikaarra omuntebe y’omuntu§ atakuragiire  kugii-
kaarraamu.
Never sit in someone’s chair before he allows you to do so.

Negative (plural)

Mulekege kwikaliza kunywa kwonka.
Never spend all your time only in drinking.

ress a habitual action, e.g.

jikaraga nonjuma oku, obu ndigira nakurugaho tolimbona.
u abuse me daily, but when I leave you, you will not see me.

4. FAR FUTURE

(1) Function

This tense is used in giving commands which shall take effect beyond the
limit of the near future tense.

(2) Formation

Its positive form is identical with that of the near future and the only way to
distinguish one from the other is first to find out what its negative form is. In
the positive, like in the near future, the command is conditioned by the
fulfilment in far future, of another action expressed by a subordinate clause
beginning with obu ‘when, if’. The negative takes an inverted prefix -ta
followed by the far future tense prefix -li-**, e.g.

(a) Positive

Obu olimubona omunsabire ebitabu byange.
When you see him ask him for my books.

Obu muliba nimuruga Kampala muleetwe ogwa Mbarara.
When travelling from Kampala come via Mbarara.

Kkyamukaaga inywena mugende Buhinga kusunga omupiira.
All of you should go to Buhinga on Saturday to watch the football match.

Obu muliba muhiigire mwite buli kisoro kyona ekiriibwa.
When you go hunting kill every animal eaten by man.

‘5

Some Runyoro-Rutooro grammarians take a different view of this tense, for
example, Maddox’s view is that the far future imperative has the same form as the
far future indicative (see An Elementary Lunyoro Grammar, London 1902:
SPCK: pp. 23). The argument here is that if that was the case the negative form of
the far future imperative would not have differed from that of the far future
indicative. In any case it is most unlikely that the positive form of the far future
indicative expresses a command. Compare: Hati mugende muliija ijo ‘You may
now go, you will come back tomorrow’ and Hati mugende baitu ijo mwije ‘You

may now go but come back tomorrow’. It is a fact, however, that the far future
imnerative malkec ice afa clanca cantaininga a -vark in the far Latirs tnd nndicra

his expresses customary action. It has no tense prefix but requires only

(2) Relative phrases

There are many flying birds (Lit. ‘The birds which fly are many’).
(b) Negative
Atakugonza tahuliiriza ngaabu yaawe.
One who does not love you hardly listens to your heroic recitation.

Ekitagamba tikirema agamba.
A speechless thing does not defeat a human being.

Enjura etahinda niyo egwa.
It is that rain which has not threatened that falls.

2. PRESENT IMPERFECT

This tense indicates that the action is still continuing or is imperfect in
present time. Its tense prefix is ni- before a consonant or the vowel i; it is na-
before the vowel a, ne- before the vowel e and no- before the vowel o.

In the negative the tense prefix is ruku-*°. It is similar to that of positive
relative phrases and comes immediately after the pronominal prefix or

” This is probably the verb -li + the infinitive prefix ku- (-liku = -ruku). It is likely
that originally the verb -li was used in all forms of the present imperfect tense, in
both direct and relative phrases. But as time went on, to differentiate between
positive and negative forms in direct phrases and between direct and relative
phrases the positive forms of direct phrases employed the copula ni- which is an
alternative to the verb -li. Thus ndikulya ‘I am eating’ a form still used by some
Runyoro-Rutooro speakers in Tooro became nindya. Nkulya, a form used by
some Runyoro-Rutooro speakers in Bunyoro is probably the result of further
sound change which mav he ilhictrated as followe:

icord both in the direct and relative phrases. The negative prefix ti-

) Positive

In Tooro, -liku became -ruku:

Arukunteera. He is beating me.
In Bunyoro, -ruku became -ku:
Okukora ki? What are you doing?

Nkusoma ekitabu kyange. I am reading my book.

NOTES ON THE PRESENT INDEFINITE, PRESENT-IMPERFECT AND PRESENT-PERFECT —

TENSES |
ertain phrases in these three tenses, in addition to their respective tense ;
refixes (in case of present indefinite and present imperfect tenses) or suffix —
n case of the Present Perfect tense), may incorporate the tense particle -kya
y impart to themselves some new meanings. q

|) Present indefinite

Vhen the tense particle -kya- is incorporated in the negative form of this

‘nse, the tense indicates that the customary action of a person or thing has, —
t a certain time, been discontinued either temporarily or permanently. The

article comes immediately after the personal prefix or concord, e.g.

1) Direct phrases (negative form)

Biro binu takyanywa maarwa.
He no longer drinks beer these days.

Abantu abaingi tibakyagenda kiro.
Many people no longer travel at night. 1

Abaana tibakyaliisa mbuzi kwonka.
Children no longer only graze goats. ‘

b) Relative phrases (negative form)

Atakyanywa maarwa ni Babinyaga.
It is Babinyaga who no longer drinks beer.

D. The indicative mood 225

Abatakyagenda kiro nubo baingi biro binu.
People who no longer travel at night are the more these days.

Abaana abatakyaliisa mbuzi nubo abo bonka abali omumasomero.
Children who no longer graze goats are only those at school.

(2) Present

When the positive form of this tense incorporates the tense particle -kya- the
tense indicates that the action which ought to have been discontinued is still

going on at the time of speaking, e.g.

Imperfect

Direct phrases (positive form)
Engoma niikyagamba.
Drums are still sounding.

Omwana naakyarra.
The child is still crying.

Enjura neekyagwa.
The rain is still falling. (It’s still raining)

Nookyahaata.
You are still peeling.

Nibyakyaliisa.
They are still grazing.

Note that this form is sometimes regarded as a separate tense under the name
Still tense.

(3) Present perfect

When the negative form of this tense incorporates the tense particle -kya the
lense indicates that the action which was to be completed has now, due to
some circumstances, been abandoned, e.g.

Orugoye rwange tinkyarukuhaire.
[ am no longer going to give you the cloth I had promised to give you.

Takyagenzire.
He is no longer with the idea of going.

cuba® or kuba ‘if’, ken verb in the apodosis takes the conditional tense

he nasal n; or -aku i after a pronominal prefix or concord represented by

he protasis is introduced by obu ‘if, when’, the verb in the protasis takes

conditional tense prefix -ku- while that in the apodosis takes the

te that in English both categories of sentences are translated in the same

Positive form with kakuba or kuba

Kakuba/kuba ompaire omulimo nkugukozire.
If you gave me some work I should do it.

Kakuba/kuba otuhaire omulimo twakugukozire.
If you gave us some work we should do it.

kakuba is nothing but k ube with the particle ka- added to it. The particle is no
doubt the shortened form of leka ‘leave’ used interjectionally to express extre-
mity. Thus the extremity in  Leka caali! ‘knba mmusangireho nkuliire ndemwa
‘that man is so good that if I found him there I should eat to my fill’ is expressed
simply by kakuba muusangireho nkuliire ndemwa.

There seems to be some variations as to the formation of conditional sentences in
the present-perfect tense amongst Runyoro-Rutooro speakers. For example, some
people in Tooro instead of having two categories of conditional tense prefix they
have only one category, -ooku-, for all the persons. Thus instead of nkugukozire
‘I should do it? and twakugukozire ‘we should do it’ they say nookugukozire
and twokugukozire respectively. Similarly, while some people in Bunyoro will
use the conditional tense prefix -ku- after a pronominal prefix or concord
represented by either a vowel or the nasal n, after the pronominal prefix or
concord represented by a consonant plus a vowel they will use -uku-. Thus in»
stead of twakugukozire they will say tuukugukozire. When Maddox and
Caumartin give -ku as the only conditional tense prefix for the Present-perfect
tense (see An Elementary Lunyoro Grammar by H.E. Maddox, pp. 29, and
Grammar ey Orunyoro by A. Caumartin, pp. 204) they apparently overlook the
vowel u which some people in Bunyoro, who use the prefix -uku after a pronome
inal prefix or concord represented by a consonant plus a vowel pronounce. It may,
however, be argued that the prefix -aku- which is here recorded is a latest devel

anment

F. The conditional mood 249

(c) Negative form with kakuba

Kakuba/kuba otampaire mulimo tinkukozire.
If you did not give me some work I should be idle.

Kakuba/kuba otatuhaire mulimo titwakukozire.
If you did not give us some work we should be idle.

(d) Positive form with obu

Obu okumpaire omulimo nkugukozire.
If you gave me some work I should do it.

Obu okutuhaire omulimo twakugukozire.
If you gave us some work we should do it.

(e) Negative form with Obu

Obu otaakumpaire mulimo tinkukozire.
If you did not give me some work I should be idle.

(f) Notes

(i) Conditional sentences in aa simple near-past are formed by adding the
suffix -ge on the verb stem in the protasis and must be introduced only by
kakuba or kuba, e.g.

Kakuba/kuba/ompairage omulimo nkugukozire.
If you gave me some work I should do it.

Kakuba/kuba/otampairege mulimo tinkokozire kantu.
If you did not give me some work I should not do anything.

(ii) If the suffix -ge is added to the verb stem in the apodosis the sentence
expresses what might happen occasionally in the future if what is supposed
did happen, e.g.

Kakuba/kuba/ompaire omulimo nkukwesirege taata.
If you gave me a job I should always regard you as my father.

In other sentences of this kind, however, the protasis may be followed by
another protasis introduced by obu and containing a verb stem to which the
suffix -ge is added, e.g.

Kakuba ontwaire, obu okumpairege omulimo gwawe, nkugukozire.
If you took me, every time you gave me your work I should do it.
(Lit. If you took me, if you gave me your work I should always do it.)

Since the verbs -ina and -li have only one tense, the Present-imperfect tense,
their conditions cannot, therefore, be expressed in simple tenses of this
mood. They are only expressed in compound tenses (see pp. 269f), though in
conditions expressed by verbs without a conditional tense prefix their simple
form may be used. See pp. 280ff.

3, CONDITIONS EXPRESSED BY VERBS IN SIMPLE TENSES WITHOUT A CONDITIONAL TENSE
PREFIX

Many conditions in Runyoro-Rutooro may be expressed by verbs without
conditional tense prefixes. Sentences containing such conditions are called
conditional sentences because they do not only express conditions on which
an action is, or would be, or would have been, or will be done, but when
translated into English their translation consists of conditional clauses
beginning with ‘if. For the purpose of comparison both, those in simple and
those in compound tenses, have been discussed together on pp. 272ff.

--- page3 ---
CHAPTER FOURTEEN

PARTICIPLES AND AUXILIARY VERBS

A. PARTICIPLES

These are verbs in the present-imperfect, the not-yet, the not-still and the
virtual-present tenses used with the verb okuba ‘to be’, in compound tenses
(for compound tenses see chapter fifteen on pp. 259ff) or with any other verb
in simple tenses so as to indicate the state in which something is, the manner
in which an action is done or the time when it was done.

Participles may, therefore, be referred to as the present participle, perfect
participle, the not-yet participle, the not-still participle and the virtual-
present participle.

In compound tenses a participle is the principal or main verb in a sentence
and must take the negative prefix. In simple tense, however, a participle may
or may not take a negative prefix. The negative prefix taken by a participle
must always be inverted.

1. PRESENT PARTICIPLE
(1) Positive

Nkasiiba ningada izooba lyagwa.
I suffered from morning till sunset.
(Lit. I spent the whole day suffering until the sun set.

(2) Negative

Nkasiiba ntarukugada.
Ntasiibe ningada.
I did not spend the day suffering.

2. PERFECT PARTICIPLE

(1) Positive

Nsangire agenzire.
I have found him gone.

CHAPTER FIFTEEN

COMPOUND TENSES

These are tenses which are used to define closely the time of action or state.
They are based on primary tenses of the verb okuba ‘to be’, functioning as
auxiliary to the verbs -ina and -li®’ or to any other verb in an appropriate
participle. (For the primary tenses of the verb okuba, and participles and
auxiliary verbs see pp. 244ff and p. 255 respectively.

Both the verb okuba and the principal verb requires the subject prefix or
concord, but only the auxiliary verb may take a relative concord.

Except in negative interrogative forms, where the negative prefix ti- is taken
by the verb okuba, the negative prefix for a compound tense is always
inverted and must be taken only by the principal verb.

Any Runyoro-Rutooro mood capable of having a simple tense may also have
a number of compound tenses. But it should be noted that the imperative and
the subjunctive moods have only one compound tense, the continuous-
present tense, used only in the positive form.

Examples of compound tenses in their various moods are given below:

A. THE IMPERATIVE MOOD
Singular Plural

Baga noogenda. Mubege nimugenda.
‘Get going I shall find you on the way.’ ‘Get going, I or we shall find
you on the way.’

$7 The verb -li may be used by some Runyoro-Rutooro speakers as auxiliary verb to
itself or to another verb in the compound tense of the far-past instead of the verb
okuba. Similarly the verb okuba may function as an auxiliary verb to itself.

THE CONDITIONAL MOOD

sliminary information about this mood has already been given on pp,
7ff. In this chapter only examples of conditional sentences in compound

E. The conditional mood 267

tenses of the conditional mood are being given. They are putting into
account conditional sentences containing verbs with a conditional tense
prefix as well as conditional sentences containing a verb without a
conditional tense prefix.

It must be pointed out, however, that in addition to the words: kakuba or
kuba ‘if’, and obu ‘if, when’, which in simple tenses introduce the protasis
of conditional sentences containing a verb with a conditional tense prefix, in
compound tenses the protasis of the same sentences may also be introduced
by the word kakusangwa or kusangwa ‘if’.

In compound tenses when the protasis of a conditional sentence is introduced
by the word obu it is the auxiliary verb okuba ‘to be’ in the protasis which
takes the conditional tense prefix just as does the verb in the apodosis. In
negative forms the verbs okuba retains this conditional tense prefix, but it
does not take the conditional negative tense prefix. This is taken by the
principal verb.

In some cases the auxiliary verb okuba may be used both in the protasis and
the apodosis. But when this is done the sentences imply that the action, if a
condition were fulfilled could be completed at a certain time or at the
moment of speaking. Examples:

1. PRESENT PERFECT TENSE INCLUDING THE NEAR-PAST
(1) Simple forms
(a) Positive form with kakuba, kuba, kakusangwa and kusangwa

Kakuba

Kuba obaire(ge) ompaire omulimo
Kakusangwa nkugukozire/nkubaire
Kusangwa ngukozire.

If you had given me some work I should have done it.

Kakuba

kuba obaire(ge) otuhaire omulimo
Kakusangwa twakugukozire/twakubaire
Kusangwa tugukozire.

If you had given us some work we should have done it.

KINDS OF NOUNS
plications of names given to things concrete or abstract give rise to the

lowing kinds of nouns:

PROPER NOUNS

sse are names used for an individual person, thing, place, country, town, —

. so as to differentiate it from other members of the class, e.g.

) Names of people

Rwakaikara, Kaikara, Kaaheeru, Rwomubitooke, Peetero,
Yohaana, Sesiria, Katalina, Mwajuma, etc.

) Names of things

Obwegendaho (name that one can give to his spear, dog, etc.)
Muziizi (name of river)
Kahunge (name of mountain, etc.)

}) Names of places

Butiiti, Bujumbura, Mparo, Weekomiire, Kyegeegwa, Bujuni, etc.

4) Names of countries
Bunvoro. Tooro, Nkole, Kigezi, Rwanda, Kiziba, etc.

B. Kinds of nouns 171

(5) Names of towns
Kaseese, Masindi, Kabarole, Hoima, Mbarara, etc.

Note that proper nouns begin with a capital letter.

2. COMMON NOUNS

These are names that can be used for any member of a class, e.g.

omuntu ‘person’, ekisoro ‘animal, beast’, orusozi ‘mountain’,
ekisaaru ‘river’, omutoma ‘back cloth’, etc.

Note that a common noun is never started with a capital letter unless it is at
the beginning of a sentence or used in a title.

3, CONCRETE NOUNS

These are names of things with form, shape, size, weight, colour or solidity.
They, at the same time may be proper or common nouns, ¢.g.

ekitabu ‘bed, book’, entebe ‘chair’, enju ‘house’, oruguudo ‘road,
street’, ibaale ‘stone’, amaizi ‘water’, Ngangi (name of a mountain),
Mpanga (name of river), etc.

4, ABSTRACT NOUNS

These are names of some quality, action or state. They are often formed from
cither other nouns or adjectives.

They, too, may either be proper or common nouns, e.g.

(i) Formed from nouns

obusuma ‘theft’, isuma ‘greedy longing for food’, ekisuma ‘greed’
from omusuma ‘thief’;

oburogo ‘witchcraft’, irogo ‘charm, spell’, okuroga ‘to charm,
bewitch’ from omurogo ‘wizard, witch’, etc.

(ii) Formed from adjectives

oburungi ‘goodness’, irungi ‘beauty that is no longer valued by other
people’ from omurungi ‘good person’;

obubi ‘wickedness, badness’, akabi ‘danger’, ekibi ‘sin’ from omubi
‘bad, wicked person’, etc.

--- page3 ---
72 Chapter ten: Some more information about nouns

. COLLECTIVE NOUNS
‘hese are names which are singular in form but standing for many
ndividuals. They are not many in Runyoro/Rutooro, but the following will
e found:
ekitebe ‘group’, igana ‘herd’, omuzinga ‘swarm’, eka ‘home, family ]
oruganda ‘clan’, ihanga ‘country’, orukurato ‘council, parliament,
meeting’, ihe ‘army’, etc.
Note that a collective noun keeps the concordial agreement of the class to
vhich it belongs but not that of the individuals it stands for, e.g.

Igana, ly’ente ze lyonere omusiri gwange.
His herd of cattle has grazed in my garden.

CHAPTER ELEVEN

THE VERB —
OVERVIEW OF ITS STRUCTURAL ELEMENTS

A. PRELIMINARY INFORMATION

Before we go into the details of the Runyoro-Rutooro verb it is necessary
first to make the following observations:

|. FORMATION OF INFINITIVE

All infinitives end in -a and take the 15th class prefix oku- (okw-), e.g.

okubaaga ‘to skin’
okwokya ‘to burn’

2. SUBJECT CONCORD

I'xcept for verbs in the imperative mood (commands) a verb in
Runyoro-Rutooro does not normally stand alone. It must be brought into
concordial agreement with the noun (expressed or understood) functioning
as the subject of the sentence by the subjectival concord, e.g.

Abahuma bagonza amata.
Herdsmen like milk.

Ebinyonyi bigonza ebijuma.
Birds like fruit.

\, OBJECT CONCORD

Similarly the verb must also be brought into concordial agreement with the
Hioun (expressed or understood) functioning as its object in the sentence by
(he objectival concord, e.g.

Amata abahuma baganywire.
The herdsmen have drunk the milk.

--- page4 ---
Chapter eleven: The verb

Ekisura ente ikiriire.
The cows have eaten the salt.

Ebijuma ebinyonyi bibisomere.
The birds have eaten the fruits.

INFLECTIONAL AND DERIVATIONAL CHANGES

ie stem undergoes changes as it expresses mood, tense, and the form of the ~
1b. Thus the verb okusara ‘to cut’ may undergo the following changes:

sara (direct command), osale (command at second hand)
sazire (perfect participle), saarra (applied form)
saza (causative form), sarwa (passive form), etc.

o this rule are the defective verbs -ta ‘how’, -ti ‘thus’, {
na” ‘have’ and -li ‘be’. The first two, sometimes referred to as particles,
ave neither mood nor tense of their own. When the subject pronominal
oncords or personal prefixes are attached to them, they express an adverbial
oncept in the mood and tense in which the verb is, which they qualify. See
yp. 52f. When the subject pronominal concords or personal prefixes are
ttached to the last two verbs a simple present-imperfect tense of the
ndicative mood is formed (see pp. 221ff). To express ‘other tenses a
>ompound form must be used. See chapter fifteen on pp. 259ff). q

he only exception t

use of the negative copula ni- is common in questions:

Is it not they who refused?

THE FIVE MOODS

ere are five moods in which the verbs in Runyoro-Rutooro may express
action connected with the noun, namely the infinitive, the imperative, the
Jicative, the subjunctive and the conditional mood.

INFINITIVE
ie infinitive mood merely names an action without mentioning any subject,
2.

okulya ‘to eat’, okuzaana ‘to play

is also used to express verbal nouns, both in positive and negative forms,

g.
Okweta kwa Ruhanga
God’s calling

Okutalya nikwija kumumara amaani.
Lack of eating will weaken him.

here is also an idea of a participle, e.g.

Kutaraaliza bagenzi baawe ogenderiire ki?
What’s your intention in not inviting your brothers?

). IMPERATIVE

The imperative mood expresses a command or entreaty, ¢.g.
Ija ‘Come!’
Nganyira ‘Forgive me!’

3. SUBJUNCTIVE
The subjunctive mood among other functions, expresses a wish, e.g.

Ogende n’obusinge.

B. The five moods — C. Verbal derivation 179

Ruhanga akulinde.
May God protect you.

Muraale kurungi.
May you sleep well.

4. INDICATIVE
The indicative mood states a fact or asks a question, e.g.

Abahuma banywa amata.
Herdsmen drink milk.

Omwana arukurra ogu abaire ki?
What’s wrong with the child who is crying?

5. CONDITIONAL

The conditional mood expresses a condition on which an action is, or should
be or should have been, or will be done, e.g.

Kuba oizire kara okumusangire.
If you came early you would find him/her.

Kuba otabaireho baakumutiire.
If you were not there they would beat him/her.

C. VERBAL DERIVATION

1. GENERAL REMARK

When the verb is in its original forms it is referred to as simple verb, but if

its stem has been extended by means of suffixes it is referred to as derived or
derivative verb, e.g.

Simple verb

okubaaga ‘to skin’
okulima ‘to cultivate’

Derivative verb

Okubaagira ‘to skin for’ okulimira ‘to cultivate for’
okubaagisa ‘to skin with’ okulimisa ‘to cultivate with’
okubaagwa ‘to be skinned’ _okulimwa ‘to be cultivated’

Chapter eleven: The verb

VERVIEW OVER DERIVATIVE VERBS

re are about eight kinds of derivative verbs in Runyoro-Rutooro, namely:

Prepositional, e.g.
okugabira ‘to give to’
okutegera ‘to trap for’
Passive, €.g.
okugabwa ‘to be given away’
okutegwa ‘to be trapped’
Causative, e.g.
okugabisa ‘to give away with property’
okutegesa ‘to trap with’
| Neuter, e.g.
okusoboka ‘to be manageable, possible’
okueweka ‘to be breakable’
) Conversive, €.g.
okubambura ‘to unpeg, to pull up” ns
okulegura ‘to remove flour after grinding
) Associative or reciprocal, e.g.
okujumangana ‘to abuse on another’
okusekerangana ‘to smile at one another
') Reflexive, €.g.

okwefoora ‘to turn oneself into’
okwesereka ‘to hide oneself?
okwebundaaza ‘to humble oneself’

3) Reduplication, e.g.

okutematema ‘to cut at close intervals
okusekaseka ‘to laugh unnecessarily’

). THE FORMATION OF THE PERFECT TENSE

The stems of both simple and derived verbs are capable of being modified to
‘orm the perfect tense. But suffixes for their modification vary according to_

D. The formation of the perfect tense 181

the syllable in which the verb ends, the form of the verb and the number of
syllables it contains, e.g.

1. SIMPLE VERBS

(1) Simple monosyllabic stems form their perfect by adding -ire to the
simple form, e.g.

6 x

ba ‘be pf. -baire
ha ‘give’ pf. -haire
ta ‘put’ pf. -taire

But note:

sa ‘grind’, pf. -siire; fa ‘die’, pf. -fiire, which pronunciation has led some
Runyoro-Rutooro speakers to pronounce -taire as -tiire, thus confusing ta
‘put’ with teera ‘beat’ of which the perfect is -tiire.

(2) Monosyllabic stems containing y- or w-compounds form their perfects
by changing the final -a to -ire, e.g.

cwa ‘break’ pf. -cwire
gya ‘go’ pf. -giire
hwa ‘get finished’ pf. -hoire
hya ‘get cooked’ pf. -hiire

Note the restoration of original vowels in -hoire from hoa, -hwa and hiire,
from hia, -hya, etc.

(3) Disyllabic stems ending in -ra which is preceded by a short vowel sound
and all verb stems ending in -nda, and -ja which is preceded by the vowel i,
replace the last two letters by -zire (see also p. 28) e.g.

bara ‘count’ pf. -bazire
rra®” ‘cry, weep’ pf. -lizire
ija ‘come’ pf. -izire
genda ‘go’ pf. -genzire
(4) Disyllabic stems ending in -na which is preceded by a long a and also
those ending in -ra which is preceded by a long vowel sound but which is

not of a prepositional origin, form their perfect by replacing the vowel
before and after them by i and e respectively, e.g.

(2) Passive verbs
(a) General rule

Formation of the perfects of these verbs becomes easier if first one is able to
put the verb into its active form and secondly give the active form of the
same verb its perfect. For example if one is given the form heebwa ‘be
given’ before one gives out its modified form it will be necessary for one to
put this form first into its active form which is ha ‘give’. The perfect of ha is
haire. From this form we get -hairwe, the modified form of -heebwa. Thus
the perfect of most of the passive ilo is a by inserting -w before the
final vowel of their modified active stem

E. TRANSITIVE AND INTRANSITIVE

-sobokere
-bambwire
-jumangaine
-eserekere
-tematemere

A simple verb in Runyoro-Rutooro may be said to have a transitive or an
intransitive meaning. A transitive verb may, however, function as an in-
transitive while an intransitive also functions as a transitive verb.

1. SENTENCES WITH TRANSITIVE VERBS

Abanyoro balima muno enkolimbo.

The Banyoro are the chief growers of pigeons peas.

Asingire omusango.
He has won the case.

Muhuurre enduuru.
Hear the alarm being raised.

2. SENTENCES WITH INTRANSITIVE VERBS

Omwana ogwijagiire.
The child is sleeping.

Mwije tugende.
Come let’s go.

Akagya kutabaara.
He went to war.

3. SENTENCES WITH TRANSITIVE VERBS USED INTRANSITIVELY

Omukazi naalima kurungi.
The woman is digging well.

--- page10 ---
Nibarora.
They are awake.

Abaana boona basingire.
All the children have passed.

ENTENCES WITH INTRANSITIVE VERBS USED TRANSITIVELY

Newijagiire oturo turungi.

I slept soundly.

Ngenzire oruhanda rutali rurungi.
I travelled a journey of misfortune.

Akantabaara ntaina kintu mukozire.
He attacked me for nothing.

CHAPTER TWELVE

DERIVATIVE VERBS IN PARTICULAR

A. PREPOSITIONAL”
1. GENERAL FUNCTIONS

These are simple or derived verb stems to which certain suffixes have been
added with the result that the action of their simple form is directed or
extended to:

(1) Person or object

The verb thus extended shows the person or thing to or for whom or for
which the action is done and thus making such a person or thing its object,
e.g.

okuboha ‘to tie’
okutema ‘to cut’
okukinga ‘to shut’

okubohera ‘to tie for’
okutemera ‘to cut for’
okukingira ‘to shut for’

Ataakugendere ruhanda akubohera entanda y’ebyenju.
The person whom you will not travel with packs ripe bananas for you as
the provision for your journey.

Gwabaga mulimo gw’omusaija okutemera mukazi iwe ekiteme obu
yabaga naagya kulima oburo.

It used to be the duty of a husband to cut down the bush for his wife
whenever she embarked on the cultivation of millet.

Kingira enkoko obwire bwaira.
Shut the chicken house now that it is getting late.
(Lit. Shut for the hens since the night is getting dark.)

" These verbs have been called prepositional by some Bantu grammarians simply

because when translated into English their translation in many cases contains a
jrepositional meaning. Because of some other meanings expressed by them, some
uiammarians prefer the term applied to prepositional. According to how
eonvenient it ig either term has been used in this book.

H. NOUNS OF CLASSES 7 AND 8

1. CLAss 7 NOUNS

The nouns of this class take eki- before a consonant or the vowel -i-, and ky-
before other vowels. They may denote animate or inanimate objects or

abstracts, e.g.
(1) Animate objects

ekibbo ‘spirillum tick’ ekihoiholya ‘butterfly’
ekihuguhugu ‘bat’ ekihukuzi ‘mole’

(2) Inanimate objects
ekibanja ‘site’ ekisiika ‘wall, room’
ekibindi ‘bowl’ ekiibo ‘basket’
ekibbira ‘hall, Royal Court’ ekibbaali ‘spirit shine’

(3) Abstracts
ekiniga ‘anger’ ekyemanyo ‘pride’
ekididi ‘amusement’ ekidindimo ‘panic’
ekifubiro ‘punishment’ ekihaso ‘shooting pain’

Persons suffering from disability are often found in this class, e.g.

ekigeja half-witted ‘person’ ekihemahemii ‘fool’
ekihunanazi ‘stupid, dull’ ekihungu ‘spendthrift’

Note:

¢kiyengo ‘a member of a household other than a house owner’
ekihagi ‘brave leader’

ckicwambete ‘a plump little child’

¢kicuucuru ‘a child of three or four years old’.

lilicate magnitude, affection and sometimes contempt, e.g.

(4) Magnitude

Wit becomes difficult for you to ascertain whether or not a noun of Class 9
ls an initial vowel only as its prefix, substitute any of the prefixes eki-,

--- page4 ---
26 Chapter seven: The noun class system

yru- or aka- for its apparent prefix. If the noun has no class prefix the initial
rowel will disappear but not the apparent prefix, e.g.

enyindo ekinyindo orunyindo akanyindo

enyonyi ekinyonyi orunyonyi akanyonyi

enyungu ekinyungu orunyungu akanyungu

enyomyo ekinyomyo orunyomyo akanyomyo

This method may not apply to nouns of class 9, which in themselves imply
magnitude, e.g.
enyanja (not ekinyanja nor orunyanja, but akanyanja is possible).

Note that though ekinyanja ‘pond, pool’ is apparently related to enyanja its
prefix eki- is not a substitute for the prefix en- on the word enyanja.
Ekinyanja introduces a new word of smaller size than enyanja.

The prefix substitute method also helps to find out the stems of some Class 9 —
nouns, e.g.

empisi ‘hyaena’, ekihisi, oruhisi, akahisi

endiiro ‘basket for millet meal’, ekiriiro, oruliiro, akaliiro.

(a) Probably more animals occur in this class than
though there are many nouns of inanimate objects too.

(b) This class is also used for abstract ideas and for verbal nouns indicating
the action itself, e.g.

in the other classes,

encunda ‘churning’, endima ‘cultivation’, | enjwara ‘dressing’

A few foreign words, however, may not be preceded by an initial vowel but
many of such words normally belong to Class 9a, (see Class 9a on pp.
128ff), e.g.

Bbaasi ‘bus’, sente ‘cents’, pikipiki ‘motor cycle’, etc.
(d) The en- prefix may also be substituted for the normal class prefix of a

noun to indicate a person plying a trade or profession, or possessing some

(e) Like omu-, when added to a simple stem of the verb, the prefix en-
expresses a relative meaning, ¢.g.

entaahamagenyi (one who frequently goes to wedding parties, as in:
Entaahamagenyi egenda eriire. An attendant of wedding parties eats
before he/she goes to attend one of them.)

engenderakulya (one whose only intention of visiting is to find food
where one has visited, as in: Engenderakulya amalembo egira make.
He/she whose only intention of visiting is to find food where he/she has
visited gets very few homes to visit to.)

embungabungi (one who is always visiting, idler, as in: Embungabungi
eseesa ebisusu. A person who is always visiting helps with disposition of
pods because time to dispose of them comes while he/she is present and
having been one of the people who have removed beans, peas, etc., from
them he/she is inevitably asked to help with their disposition.)
entakabonaga (one who has never seen anything very striking)

2. CLAss 10 NOUNS

Here may be found the plural forms of all Class 9 nouns capable of taking a
plural. (But see also Class 6).

The class prefix is identical with that of Class 9, but the two classes differ
greatly in that each has its own concordial agreement, e.g.
Class 9:

ente enu ‘this cow’
embuzi neerya obunyansi. ‘The goat is eating grass.’

Class 10:

ennte zinu ‘these cows’
embuzi nizirya obunyansi. ‘The goats are eating grass.’

Class 10 also functions as the plural of words in Class 11, e.g.

Class 11:
orugoye ‘cloth’, plural engoye (class 10)

But sound change often takes place here (see Class II), e.g.

orubengo ‘lower millstone’, pl. emengo
orulimi ‘tongue, language’, pl. endimi

Very often it also functions as the plural of some words in Class 9a capable
of taking a plural (see Class 9a), e.g. :
Class 9a:

stookingi enu nungi ‘this stocking is nice’
Class 10:

stookingi zinu nungi ‘these stockings are nice’

J. NOUNS OF CLASSES 9A AND 10A
1. CLAss 9A NOUNS

(1) Formation

These are nouns which have neither a class prefix nor an Initial Vowel, but
which have the same concordial agreement as Class 9 nouns. Foreign words,
names of colours, grasshoppers, domestic animals, places, towns, countries,
rivers and lakes or seas are usually found here.

J. Nouns of classes 9a and 10a 129

The following are the typical examples. Note that there are nouns beginning
with mu- and ki-; these must not be confused with the prefixes of Classes 1,
3 and 7.

(2) Semantic properties
(a) Foreign words

bbaasi ‘bus’
gavumenti ‘government’

motoka ‘motor car’
kalenda ‘calendar’

Ofiisi ‘office’
kamera ‘camera’

(b) Colours

Names of colour in Runyoro-Rutooro are usually based on concrete things
such as vegetation, soil and domestic animals including fowls. Thus you
have names such as:

kinyansi ‘like grass, that is green’, kijubwa ‘like grass called ejubwa, that
is green’, rubabi ‘plantain leaf, that is green’, kitaka ‘like soil, that is
brown’, kataiki ‘like a hill made by edible termites of smaller size, that is
light brown’, kyenju ‘like a ripe banana, that is yellow’, kihuukya ‘like the
ripe seeds of “ehuukya” ‘a plant with purple berries, that is purple’, kigaaju
‘like a reddish brown cow, that is red’, kyeru ‘like a white cow or hen, that
is white’, kisiina ‘like a dark brown cow, that is dark brown’, kibuubi ‘like
i greyish cow, that is grey’, kikara ‘like a black cow, hen, etc., that is
black’, etc.

Modernity has brought about the following colours:

kaneke ‘dark blue cotton cloth, that is blue’

bbururu ‘blue’

kakobe ‘purple; suspected to have originated from the colour of the hind
part of enkobe, a Runyankore-Rukiga word for a ‘baboon’.

(cv) Names of grasshoppers

kijubwa ‘green grasshopper’, kibaizi ‘grasshopper with red on back and
wings, believed to be the leader of the swarm’, cf. omubaizi ‘carpenter,
joiner’ and also omuheesi ‘smith, the word for kibaizi in Runyankore-
tukiga’, katikoomere ‘light brown grasshopper’.

tough cattle may have names which are based on the shape of their horns.

If it is that of Class 7 or 11, that is the place as a village (ekyaro) or town —
