(ii) Mixed prefixes

Because of an historical or geographical meaning underlying names of
places, towns and countries often take a mixture of apparent prefixes. In
most cases the prefix ki- or kya- denotes possession of the clan or person
who settled in the place first. Places or countries of some historic and
economic significance take bu- as their prefix, e.g.

Buganda, Busoga, Bagangaizi, Busongora, etc.

Kisiita (cf. Abasiita), Kikurungu (cf. Abakurungu),
Kisambu (cf. Abasambu), Kibiito (cf. Ababiito)

Kicwamba (cf. Abacwamba), Kigumba (cf. Abagumba), etc.

Kyabagweri (cf. Abagweri), Kyakikoyo (cf. Abakoyo)
Kyabanyonza (cf. Abanyonza), Kyabaliisa (cf. Abaliisa)
Kyamugenyi (cf. the name of Mugenyi), etc.

(iii) Examples of mixed prefixes

Names of places

Rwamwanja, Bwanika, Kakabara, Ibonde, Bwabya, etc.

Names of towns
Kabarole, Hoima, Katwe, Masindi, Kasenyi, etc.
Names of countries
Bunyoro, Tooro, Kigezi, Mubende, Rwanda, etc.
Names of rivers including lakes

Mubuku, Mpanga, Muziizi, Kafu, Kyoga (Kioga), etc.

K. NOUNS OF CLASS 11

1. FORMATION

All the nouns in this class except names of mountains take the prefix oru-
before a consonant and orw- before a noun stem beginning with a vowel,
Ge

orubabi ‘plantain leaf’, orubaju ‘side, rib’

oruhara ‘baldness’, orukanga ‘framework of house’, etc.

orwamba ‘blood of dead animal’, orwigi ‘door’

orwemo ‘place in a house where people eat’, orwoma ‘wire’

2, SEMANTIC PROPERTIES

The oru- and orw- prefixes may also be substituted for the normal class
prefix of a noun to indicate an augmentative or pejorative idea, e.g.

orusaija ‘a clumsy person’ ef.
orukazi ‘a clumsy woman’ es
orwisiki ‘a clumsy girl’
orute ‘a clumsy cow’ a
oruti ‘a long stick’ .
orunyonyi ‘a big long bird’ ie

ente ‘ow’

enyonyi ‘bird’

See also isaija, isigazi, etc. and also eriiru and erintu on p. 120.

liefore stems of names of some countries and names of some tribes, the
prefixes indicate languages spoken in those countries or by those tribes, e.g.

Orunyoro language spoken by the people of Bunyoro

Orutooro language spoken by the people of Tooro

Oruganda language spoken by the people of Buganda

Orunyankole language spoken by the people of Nkole (Ankole)

Orungereza language spoken by the people of Bungereza
(England)

Orufuransa language spoken by the people of Bufaransa (France)

Some of the nouns in Class II may also indicate abstract notions, e.g.

orwanju ‘lewdness’, orugambo ‘rumour, chatter, empty talk’
orufu ‘death, illness’, oruceenyo ‘rheumatism’
érujali ‘merry talk’, orwiru ‘unpleasant behaviour’

Names of mountains take a mixture of apparent prefixes, e.g. Kahunge,
Hisalga, Ngangi, Musaijamukuru, Toonya, etc.

fhe nouns in this class take the prefix otu- before a noun stem beginning
vith a consonant and otw- before a vowel. These prefixes are substituted for
he normal class prefixes of nouns to indicate diminution of quantity and
ize, which otherwise may not be indicated by the prefixes of Class 12, e.g.

otuta ‘a little milk’ but never akata cf. amata ‘milk’
otwarwa ‘a little beer, dregs of beer’

but never akaarwa cf. amaarwa ‘beer’
otwizi ‘a little water, a drop of water’ but never akaizi

cf. amaizi ‘water’

2) Function

This class functions in a way as the plural of Class 12 though the normal
jlural of Class 12 is Class 14. Compare thus:

Class 12 Class 13 Class 14
akajuma ‘grain, pill’ otujuma (pl.) obujuma (pl.)
akanono ‘tiny nail, claw’ otunono (pl.) obunono (pl.)
akaara ‘tiny finger, toe’ otwara (pl.) obwara (pl.)
akanyindo ‘small nose’ otunyindo (pl.) obunyindo (pl)

3ut note:

akagita ‘a little butter’ but never *otugita cf. amagita ‘butter’
akasura ‘a little salt’ but never *otusura ” ekisura ‘salt’
akasera ‘a little porridge’ but never *otusera ” obusera ‘porridge’
akookulya “a little food’ but never *otwokulya ” ebyokulya ‘food’

yturo ‘sleep’ is the only original word in this class.

CHAPTER EIGHT

GENITIVE PARTICLES AND NYA-
IN COMBINATION WITH SPECIFIC NOUNS

A. GENITIVE PARTICLES

The Genitive particles owa, aba, ekya, ebya, eza, aka and obwa may be
added to some Runyoro-Rutooro nouns so as to give them not only new
meanings but also concordial agreement similar to that of the nouns to which
they themselves belong. But their use is often accompanied by sound
change. For Sound Change see Chapter 2.

|, OWA

The nouns to which this particle has been added belong to Class 1. They
denote:

(1) Honorific terms

‘villager’

4. WA

his particle puts the nouns to which it is added in Class la. It therefore
ives them the status of being either names of people (proper nouns) or
ames of animals which are personified, e.g.

1) Names of people (proper nouns):

wenjara ‘of famine’, wenkya ‘of sheer luck’
weeboha ‘of foxes’, wenkere ‘of toad, born premature’, etc.

2) Personified names of animals (used in story telling):

Warujojo ‘Mr. Elephant’, Waruhisi ‘Mr. Hyaena’
Wante ‘Mrs. Cow’, Wankoko ‘Mr. Cock or Mrs. Hen’, etc.

A. Genitive particles 151

See also p. 115; and also note the original prefixes very carefully in words
such as Warujojo and Wante.

As is always the case with nouns of class 1a, the plural of the nouns to which
the genitive particle wa has been added is in Class 2a. See Class 2a on pp.
115f.

15. KYA

The nouns to which this particle is added belong to either Class 7 or Class 9a
and may denote things such as:

(1) Days of the Week (Class 7 with plural in Class 8), e.g.

Kyakabiri ‘Tuesday’, Kyakasatu ‘Wednesday’
Kyakana ‘Thursday’, Kyakataano ‘Friday’
Kyamukaaga ‘Saturday’.

Note that in plural the initial vowel of the particle bya is restored:

Ebyakabiri ‘Tuesdays’, Ebyakasatu ‘Wednesdays’, etc.

(2) Places named after people who occupied them first (Class 9a with plural
in Class 10a), e.g.

Kyakakere ‘Kakere as the founder’, Kyambogo ‘Mbogo as the
founder’, Kyamusuuga ‘Musuuga as the founder’, Kyarwagonya
‘Rwagonya as the founder’, Kyabinyomo ‘Binyomo as the founder’,
Kyabigodi ‘Bigodi as the founder’, etc.

Note that no initial vowel is restored when these nouns are put in plural, e.g.

/aakyakakere mu Kisongi zingaha?
}low many places called Kyakakere are there in Kisongi?

/Zaakyabagweri zombi omu Tooro hati ikombekuwamu enganda zindi.

lsoth places in Tooro which are called Kyabagweri are now inhabited by
other clans.

(4) Names of cows in terms of names of their mothers (Class 9 with plural in
Class 10 and eza as the class prefix), e.g.

18. KA (PRONOUNCED KAA)

The class of the nouns to which this particle has been added also depends on
the underlying idea of the noun formed. The nouns denote:

(1) Names of calves in terms of names of their mothers (Class 9 with plural
in Class 10 and eza as the genitive particle representing the class), e.g.

Kaakitale ‘calf born or Kitale, the white cow’

Kaakyozi ‘calf born of Kyozi, the black cow’

Kankerenge ‘calf born of Nkerenge, the black and red cow’
Kaasiina ‘calf born of Siina, the dark brown cow’

A. Genitive particles 155

) Diminution of size as expression of sympathy or contempt in connection
with a person or thing known to the public or to both the speaker and the
person spoken to (Class 12 with plural in Class 14 and bwa as the genitive
particle prefix representing the class), e.g.

Kaarwakaikara kaaba nikakuseerra.

Rwakaikara the small one was looking for you.

Kaarutanga nako leero kabuliire kaabukunduuza.

Even Rutanga, the small he-goat, has eaten the grass to his satisfaction.
Kaakahogo manyeki kurugayo omukisaka kiro kya hati.

It’s doubtful as to whether poor Kahogo, ‘the reddish calf,’ will return
from pasture to night.

Kaarediyo ke nikagamba muno okukira rediyo enkooto.

His little radio is louder than a big radio.

Kaamotoka ke amazire na ko ebiro bingi.

He has spent many days with his little car.

Kaawakame kakairuka kaasiga Warugo.

Mr. Wakame the small ran faster than Mr. Warugo.

(3) Personal name derived from ancestor or God whom the parents paid
devotion to before getting the child or to places and circumstances in which
the children were born (usually both males and females have similar names
which belong to Class 1a with plural in Class 2a, e.g.

Kaawamara ‘of the Mucwezi Wamara’
Kaamulindwa ‘of the Mucwezi Mulindwa’
Kaakyomya ‘of the Mucwezi Kyomya’
Kaaheeru ‘born outside the house’

Koomwiswa ‘born in long grass’, etc.

See also pp. 109f.

THE PARTICLE NYA- (-NYA-)

ny nouns and phrases in Runyoro-Rutooro may take the particle nya-
e, of, who’ either as their pre-prefix or as their infix to denote:

People’s habits, behaviour, characteristics, etc. (Class 1a with plural in
ass 2a), €.g.
nyagaarr, ‘a person who cries unnecessarily’

nyantagambirwa ‘a disobedient person’

CHAPTER NINE

FORMATION OF NOUNS

A. LIST OF PREFIXES AND SUFFIXES

Nouns in Runyoro-Rutooro may be formed by:

1. ADDING A CLASS PREFIX TO A NOMINAL OR ADJECTIVAL ROOT, ©.8.

omu + ntu = omuntu ‘person’
obu + rung = oburungi ‘gooness’

2. ADDING A CLASS PREFIX TO A VERBAL ROOT OR STEM, €.8.

omu + lim + i= omulimi ‘cultivator’
obu + lim + e = obulime ‘land tilled but not sown’

3. ADDING THE PRONOMINAL CONCORDS KI-, KA~ OR RU- TO A VERBAL ROOT OR STEM
>
e.g.

ki + teera = kiteera ‘one who beats’

ka + jaika = kajaika ‘one who wanders about’

ru + bunduguza = rubunduguza ‘one who travels early in the
morning’

4, ADDING THE PARTICLE NYA- TO A NOUN

Here the original noun loses its initial vowel though its original class is
maintained, e.g.

nya + mukazi = nyamukazi ‘the woman both of us know’
nya + motoka = nyamotoka ‘the car both of us know’
§, ADDING A CLASS PREFIX AND THE PARTICLE NYA~ TO A NOUN

The original noun loses not only its initial vowel but also its original class
6.2.

». Cass 13 Nouns

1) Formation

fhe nouns in this class take the prefix otu- before a noun stem beginning
vith a consonant and otw- before a vowel. These prefixes are substituted for
he normal class prefixes of nouns to indicate diminution of quantity and
ize, which otherwise may not be indicated by the prefixes of Class 12, e.g.

otuta ‘a little milk’ but never akata cf. amata ‘milk’
otwarwa ‘a little beer, dregs of beer’

but never akaarwa cf. amaarwa ‘beer’
otwizi ‘a little water, a drop of water’ but never akaizi

cf. amaizi ‘water’

2) Function

This class functions in a way as the plural of Class 12 though the normal
jlural of Class 12 is Class 14. Compare thus:

Class 12 Class 13 Class 14

akaara ‘tiny finger, toe’ otwara (pl.) obwara (pl.)
akanyindo ‘small nose’ otunyindo (pl.) obunyindo (pl)

3ut note:

akookulya “a little food’ but never *otwokulya ” ebyokulya ‘food’

yturo ‘sleep’ is the only original word in this class.

CHAPTER EIGHT

GENITIVE PARTICLES AND NYA-
IN COMBINATION WITH SPECIFIC NOUNS

A. GENITIVE PARTICLES

The Genitive particles owa, aba, ekya, ebya, eza, aka and obwa may be
added to some Runyoro-Rutooro nouns so as to give them not only new
meanings but also concordial agreement similar to that of the nouns to which
they themselves belong. But their use is often accompanied by sound
change. For Sound Change see Chapter 2.

denote:

‘villager’

4. WA

his particle puts the nouns to which it is added in Class la. It therefore
ives them the status of being either names of people (proper nouns) or
ames of animals which are personified, e.g.

15. KYA

The nouns to which this particle is added belong to either Class 7 or Class 9a
and may denote things such as:

(1) Days of the Week (Class 7 with plural in Class 8), e.g.

Kyakabiri ‘Tuesday’, Kyakasatu ‘Wednesday’
Kyakana ‘Thursday’, Kyakataano ‘Friday’
Kyamukaaga ‘Saturday’.

Note that in plural the initial vowel of the particle bya is restored:

Ebyakabiri ‘Tuesdays’, Ebyakasatu ‘Wednesdays’, etc.

(2) Places named after people who occupied them first (Class 9a with plural
in Class 10a), e.g.

Kyakakere ‘Kakere as the founder’, Kyambogo ‘Mbogo as the
founder’, Kyamusuuga ‘Musuuga as the founder’, Kyarwagonya
‘Rwagonya as the founder’, Kyabinyomo ‘Binyomo as the founder’,
Kyabigodi ‘Bigodi as the founder’, etc.

Note that no initial vowel is restored when these nouns are put in plural, e.g.

/aakyakakere mu Kisongi zingaha?

other clans.

Class 10 and eza as the class prefix), e.g.

17. RWA

18. KA (PRONOUNCED KAA)

The class of the nouns to which this particle has been added also depends on
the underlying idea of the noun formed. The nouns denote:

(1) Names of calves in terms of names of their mothers (Class 9 with plural
in Class 10 and eza as the genitive particle representing the class), e.g.

Kaakitale ‘calf born or Kitale, the white cow’

Kaakyozi ‘calf born of Kyozi, the black cow’

Kankerenge ‘calf born of Nkerenge, the black and red cow’
Kaasiina ‘calf born of Siina, the dark brown cow’

A. Genitive particles 155

) Diminution of size as expression of sympathy or contempt in connection
with a person or thing known to the public or to both the speaker and the
person spoken to (Class 12 with plural in Class 14 and bwa as the genitive
particle prefix representing the class), e.g.

Kaarwakaikara kaaba nikakuseerra.

Rwakaikara the small one was looking for you.

Kaarutanga nako leero kabuliire kaabukunduuza.

Even Rutanga, the small he-goat, has eaten the grass to his satisfaction.
Kaakahogo manyeki kurugayo omukisaka kiro kya hati.

It’s doubtful as to whether poor Kahogo, ‘the reddish calf,’ will return
from pasture to night.

Kaarediyo ke nikagamba muno okukira rediyo enkooto.

His little radio is louder than a big radio.

Kaamotoka ke amazire na ko ebiro bingi.

He has spent many days with his little car.

Kaawakame kakairuka kaasiga Warugo.

Mr. Wakame the small ran faster than Mr. Warugo.

(3) Personal name derived from ancestor or God whom the parents paid
devotion to before getting the child or to places and circumstances in which
the children were born (usually both males and females have similar names
which belong to Class 1a with plural in Class 2a, e.g.

Kaamulindwa ‘of the Mucwezi Mulindwa’
Kaakyomya ‘of the Mucwezi Kyomya’

CHAPTER NINE

FORMATION OF NOUNS

A. LIST OF PREFIXES AND SUFFIXES

Nouns in Runyoro-Rutooro may be formed by:

1. ADDING A CLASS PREFIX TO A NOMINAL OR ADJECTIVAL ROOT, ©.8.

omu + ntu = omuntu ‘person’
obu + rung = oburungi ‘gooness’

2. ADDING A CLASS PREFIX TO A VERBAL ROOT OR STEM, €.8.

omu + lim + i= omulimi ‘cultivator’
obu + lim + e = obulime ‘land tilled but not sown’

3. ADDING THE PRONOMINAL CONCORDS KI-, KA~ OR RU- TO A VERBAL ROOT OR STEM
>
e.g.

ki + teera = kiteera ‘one who beats’

ka + jaika = kajaika ‘one who wanders about’

ru + bunduguza = rubunduguza ‘one who travels early in the
morning’

4, ADDING THE PARTICLE NYA- TO A NOUN

Here the original noun loses its initial vowel though its original class is
maintained, e.g.

nya + mukazi = nyamukazi ‘the woman both of us know’
nya + motoka = nyamotoka ‘the car both of us know’
§, ADDING A CLASS PREFIX AND THE PARTICLE NYA~ TO A NOUN

The original noun loses not only its initial vowel but also its original class
6.2.

The words makumi and bikumi may be dropped when counting, e.g.

20 (abiri) 300 (bisatu)
Additional figures of hundreds and thousands may be connected by means of
mu and na e.g.

‘235 cows’ is read ente bibiri mu asatu na itaano.

But mu may not be all that necessary e.g.

‘1450 trees’ may be read emiti rukumi bina n‘ataano.

2. ORDINALS

This is formed by means of the word okubanza ‘to begin, to come first’ —
preceded by an appropriate genitive particle e.g.

omwana (0)w’okubanza ‘the first child’
abantu (a)b’okubanza ‘the first men’
ibara lye ery’okubanza ‘his first name’
ekigambo (e)ky’okubanza ‘the first word’

(2) 2™ to 5%

These are formed by means of the adverbial prefix ka- attached on to the
numeral stem and then preceded by an appropriate genitive particle e.g.

omwaka (o)gwa kabiri
ekitebe (e)kya kasatu
orukumo (o)rwa kataano

‘(the) second year’
‘(the) third class’
‘(the) fifth finger’

Note that the prefix ka- is also attached to the interrogative particle

-ingaha?, e.g.
Wakamurora kaingaha? ‘How many times have you seen him?’
Yakakujuma kaingaha? ‘How many times has he abused you?’
Bakaleeta amaizi kaingaha? ‘How many times did they bring water?’

In answering these questions, the adverbial prefix ka- is used. See note (iii)
and (iv) on p. 60f. j

Yakazaara kasatu ‘she has given birth three times’
Twakagyayo kabiri ‘We have been there twice’

K. Numeral concords 81

(3) 6" to 10%

These are formed by means of cardinals (without initial vowel) preceded by
an appropriate genitive particle e.g.

ekiragiro kya mukaaga ‘the sixth commandment’
omwaka gwa musanju ‘the seventh year’

(4) Above 10th

These are formed by means of the cardinals preceded by an appropriate
genitive particle. But as in the case of cardinals, the connection of units to
the tens is by means of na. But it should be noted that unless the unit
consists of 1 the cardinals 2-5 must always take the plural numeral concord
of the noun which is mentioned, e.g.

okwezi kwa ikumi na kumu ‘the eleventh month’
omumwaka gwa ikumi ne‘taano ‘in the fifteenth year’
omurundi ogwa nsanju na musanju ‘the seventy seventh time’

(5) Fractions

he order in which fractions are expressed depends on the convenience of
the speaker. Some prefer beginning with the numerator and others prefer
beginning with denominator. If the numerator is mentioned first its prefix
will depend on the numeral used. If the numerator of a fraction consists of
(he numeral 1, the stem of that numeral will take the class 7 prefix. If it
eonsists of any of numerals 2 to 5, the stem of the numeral will take the class
NM prefix.

lhe numerator expresses the fraction of a unit and must therefore be
lullowed by a genitive particle agreeing with it. The denominator takes the
ilverbial formative prefix ka-, e.g.

oly

(6) Distributives

These are formed by reduplicating the number in question, e.g.
Abantu bazina babiri babiri.
People dance two by two.

Habisoro byona ebisemiire olitwara musanju musanju.
Of all the clean beast you will take seven and seven.

The word buli offers an alternative to the reduplication of the numbers:

Buli bantu basatu bahimbe orubbaaho rumu.
Let every three persons carry one bench.

of Muhimbe basatu basatu.

Carry (it) in threes.

L. SUFFIXES

Mention of these has been made under ‘affixes’ on p. 42. It should be know
that these are letters or syllables at the end of the roots of nearly every
Runyoro-Rutooro verb and every noun derived from it.

Those at the end of the roots of verbs determine the form of the verb in use,
its mood and tense.

Those at the end of the roots of nouns determine the meaning expressed b
the noun.

For example, all infinitives in Runyoro-Rutooro end in -a, e.g.

okulima ‘to dig, cultivate, till’
okuzaana ‘to play’

The roots of these verbs are -lim-, and -zaan- respectively. The -a at the en
of each root is a suffix which may be replaced by any other suffix accordi
to the form which one wants the verb to take. For instance, instead of th
forms lima, and zaana we may have the forms:

L. Suffixes 83

limira ‘dig for’, and zaanira ‘play for’; or limisa ‘dig with’, and zaanisa
‘play with’, etc.

Similarly, out of the verbs okulima and okuzaana we can get nouns such as
omulimi ‘cultivator’ and omuzaani ‘player’. The vowel -i at the end of each
noun is a suffix. The roots of the nouns remain lim and zaan as is the case
with the verbs from which they are derived. By a change of the suffix or
both the prefix and the suffix the nouns omulimi and omuzaani may
become omulimo ‘work’, omulima ‘professional digger’, endima ‘method
of digging’, omuzaano ‘play’, omuzaana ‘maid servant’ and enzaana
‘method of playing’ respectively.

his idea of suffixes must be borne in mind when nouns and verbs are
wtudied in detail in chapters 9, 11, 12, 13 and 15.

CHAPTER FIVE

FORMATION OF LOCATIVES

A, ADVERBIAL NOUNS AND PRONOUNS

Nouns and pronominal roots to which the locatives or adverbial formative
prefixes* omu- (omw-, omu), ha- (ha), ku-, owa- (owa) and omba are
added acquire the status of an adverb of place and may therefore be referred
\o as ‘adverbial nouns’ and ‘adverbial pronouns’ respectively.

|, AGREEMENT

As in the case of ordinary nouns and self-standing pronouns, any verb or
adjective referring to an adverbial noun or pronoun must be brought into
concordial agreement with it by means of the concordial prefix of place ha-.

l'xamples

Omunsi habi.
The world is a bad place.

Omwiguru harungi.
Heaven is a good place.

Omukikopo hafunzire okukira omusafuliya.
lhe volume of a cup is smaller than that of the sauce pan.

Omuruguudo hagazi okukira omumuhanda.
lhe road is wider than the foot path.

Owanyu hara okukira owaabu.
Your home is further than his.

Ilanu hairagwire.
Iliis spot is dirty (lit. It is dirty here).

” Some Bantu grammarians refer to the particles omu- (omw-, omu), ha- (ha),
Wie (owa) and omba as prepositions. But as Ashton, Mulira, Ndawula and
lueker, when dealing with their counter part in Luganda, put it, ‘adverbial forma-
live prefixes’ is a better description, because it also covers their non-prepositional
Hehaviour. See footnotes on A Luganda Grammar, London — New York —

2. IN COMBINATION WITH DEMONSTRATIVES

Note that although mu- and ku- are never attached to adjectival roots and
verb stems, they are, however, like ha- attached to the pronominal roots -nu_
and -li to make demonstratives, e.g.

mu + nu = munu ‘in here’

mu + li= muli ‘in there’

ha + nu = hanu ‘here’

ha + li= hali ‘there’

ku + nu = kunu ‘this way/side’

ku + li = kuli ‘that way/side’

3. IN COMBINATION WITH THE -O OF REFERENCE

As is the case with pronominal concords, when the prefixes mu- and ha- are
added to the -o of reference self-standing pronouns of place are made, e.g.

mu + 0 = mwo ‘in it’
ha + o= ho ‘on it’
Note that when -o of reference is added to ku- we get the adjective kwo
‘real’ and not a self-standing pronoun of place, e.g.

Onu muntu kwo.
This is a real person.

B. LOCATIVES AS ADVERBIAL SUFFIXES

Note that in most cases verbs relating to adverbial nouns or pronoung
mentioned or understood must have an adverbial suffix or an encliti¢
appropriate to the adverbial formative prefix of the noun or pronoun being
referred to. There are only three adverbial suffixes in Runyoro-Rutooro;
namely -mu, -ho and -yo.

B. Locatives as adverbial suffixes 89

-mu: This is used in connection with the adverbial noun or pronoun of
which omu- is the adverbial formative prefix, e.g.

Omunsi busamu kusemererwa ‘there is no happiness in the world’
Munu harumu amaizi ‘there is water in here’
Taahamu ‘Get in’

-ho: This is used in connection with the adverbial noun or pronoun of which
ha- is the adverbial formative prefix, e.g.

Hanju heemeriireho ekidongodongo ‘a heron is standing on the house’
Hansi haroho ki? ‘What is on the ground?’
Ihaho ‘take away’ Rugaho ‘get away’

Important note:

lhe -ha? at the end of words such as noorugaha? ‘Where are you coming
lrom?’, noogyaha? ‘Where are you going?’, egambiiraha? ‘Where has the
‘larm been raised?’, etc., is not an adverbial suffix but a shortened form of
the adverb nkaha? ‘Where’. See also p. 39.

yo: This is used in connection with the adverbial noun or pronoun of which
#wa- or omba or ku- is its adverbial formative prefix, and also in connection
with the demonstrative oku whether mentioned or understood, e.g.

Ndigendayo ndole enyamunungu ‘I shall go there to see the porcupine’
Owaitu haraireyo abagenyi bana ‘Four visitors slept at our home’
Omb‘Omunyoro haliyo embwa endurumi ‘There is a fierce dog at the
chief’?s home’

Kuli busayo muntu ‘There is no person there’

Muraireyo muta? ‘How did you sleep over there?’

Note the absence of oku in sentences in which nouns with omu- and ha- as
\heir adverbial formative prefixes have been used and the use of -yo below.

Obaire ogiire kwihayo ki omukibira?

What had you gone to get from the forest?

CHAPTER SIX

SENTENCES

A. SUBJECT PHRASE AND VERBAL PHRASE
|. GENERAL REMARK

In many, if not all, languages a sentence normally consists of two parts,
namely the subject and the predicate. The subject is the thing (or person)
which is spoken about and represented by either a noun or a pronoun. The
predicate is what is said about the subject and represented by a verb. Thus in
the sentence embwa ziboigora ‘Dogs bark’ the subject is embwa and the
predicate is ziboigora.

If the verb in the predicate expresses an action, the thing or person towards
which its action is directed is termed the object. Thus in the sentence
omukazi alimire omusiri ‘The woman has dug a garden’, the subject is
omukazi and the predicate is alimire omusiri. The object of the verb
alimire is omusiri.

lhe verb which takes an object, such as verb alimire in omukazi alimire
omusiri, is called transitive. That which does not need an object, such as
siboigora in embwa ziboigora, is called intransitive.

The word which completes the meaning in sentences in which the verb
okuba and li (ri, di) ‘to be’ are or should have been used is not termed the
object, but the complement, because it completes the sense, e.g.

Iinte biba bisoro by’omugaso.

Cattle are useful animals.

Iinkungani teba nungi.

Quarrelling is no good.

ikikoko kimuli omukutu.

There is wax in his ear. (Lit. wax is with him in the ear).

Omukono gumuli kubi.

His arm is paining him. (Lit. The arm is with him in a bad way).

Ukintu kindi omurubaju.

| am feeling pain in one of the ribs. (Lit. Something is with me in the rib).


--- page9 ---

B. WORDS
OBJECT

THE POSITION OF SUBJECT, PREDICATE AND

1. SuBJECT

The subject part of a sentence may contain words which give more
information about the thing or person spoken about, e.g. in the sentence
Omukazi alimire omusiri ‘The woman has dug a garden’ more informatio
may be given about the subject omukazi thus Omukazi omurungi mun
omuraira ow’enyindo esungire alimire omusiri ‘The tall extremel
beautiful woman with a pointed nose has dug a garden’.

The word omukazi in the subject part of this sentence, that is, Omukazi
omurungi muno omuraira ow’enyindo esungire, is the main one upo
which other words hang. It is therefore the subject word.

B. Words in the position of subject, predicate and object

2. PREDICATE

Likewise more information may be given about something talked about the
subject, e.g. Omukazi alimire kurungi muno omusiri okukira abakazi
abandi boon ab’omu Tooro. The word alimire in the predicate part of this
sentence is the main one on which other words are centred. It is therefore the
predicate word.

In dividing a sentence into subject and predicate, words which give more
information about the subject must be put in the subject part of the sentence
and those which give more information about the predicate word in the pre-
dicate part of the sentence. Thus in the sentence omukazi omurungi muno
omuraira ow’enyindo esungire alimire kurungi muno omusiri okukira
abakazi abandi boona ab’omu Tooro, the subject is Omukazi omurungi
muno omuraira ow’enyindo esungire and the predicate is alimire kurungi
muno omusiri okukira abakazi abandi boona ab’omu Tooro.

\, OBJECT

I) some cases more information may be given about the object of the verb.
lor instance the object omusiri in the sentence Omukazi alimire omusiri
‘The woman has dug a garden’ may be extended to Omukazi alimire
#musiri mukooto muno okukira emisiri endi yoona ey’omu Tooro ‘The
woman has dug a larger garden than other gardens in Tooro.’ Words which
i've more information about the object must be put in the object part of the

sentence when dividing it into verb and object.

!here are cases when the object of the verb is reversed, that is, instead of
voming after the verb as shown in the examples above it begins a sentence or
somes immediately after the subject. But when this happens, the verb must
lie brought into concordial relationship with both the subject and the object.
| ie objectival concord comes directly before the verb stem, e.g.

()musiri omukazi agulimire.

‘)mukazi omusiri agulimire.

lhe woman has dug the garden.

(Lil, ‘The garden the woman has dug it’ or ‘The woman the garden she has
ig it’)

Amata abahuma baganywire.

Abahuma amata baganywire.

Ihe herdmen have drunk the milk.

(Lil, ‘The milk the herdsmen they have drunk it’ or ‘The herdsmen the
Milk they have drunk it’)

5. THE VOWEL IN SIMPLE VERB STEMS

The vowel in the simple verb stem affects the sound of the vowel at the
beginning of the extension of the verb stem. Thus if the vowel in a simple
stem of the verb is a, i, or u, the vowel at the beginning of the suffix is i, if it
is e or 0, the vowel at the beginning of the suffix is e, e.g.

Simple Verb Stem Derived Verb Stem

baba ‘heat over fire’ babira, babisa, babisiza
siba ‘plait (rope)’ sibira, sibisa, sibisiza
juuka ‘run away from the herd’ juukira, juukisa, juukisiza
tega ‘trap’ tegera, tegesa, tegeseza
boha ‘tie’ bohera, bohesa, boheseza
noga ‘pick’ nogera, nogesa, nogeseza

B. SOUND CHANGE IN CONSONANTS
|. THE CONSONANTS I AND r

These two sounds belong to the same group of sounds but when one member
of the group is used for one kind of sound the other is not used, their use is
dependent on the sounds which surround them.

l is used:
(a) Before i, e, ya, ye, and yo, e.g.

okulima ‘to dig’, okuleka ‘to leave’, omulya ‘cannibal’, Lyembaire
‘name of village in Tooro’, ira lyona ‘long ago’, etc.

Unless i or e proceeds, e.g.

eryegero ‘school’, iriisizo ‘grazing ground’, etc.
In some cases, more particularly when giving a command to one person with
a verb stem beginning with I the sound d is used, e.g.

leka becomes deka ‘let alone’
lekera becomes dekera ‘let go’
leero becomes deero ‘now’
limu becomes dimu

‘is used in all other cases, e.g.

omukaro ‘dried meat’, obusera ‘gruel porridge made from millet or rice’,
okugabura ‘to marry’ (of a bridegroom), okugura ‘to buy’, etc.

l1ere again in some cases, more particularly when giving a command to one
person with a verb stem beginning with r, the sound d is used, e.g.

rugabo becomes dugabo ‘to get away’

rora becomes dora ‘see, behold’

ruhurwawe becomes duhurwawe ‘I am telling the truth’. (Said by
children to their parents as a sign of swearing. Lit. 1 ‘appeal to your skin
garment’)

Ruhanga becomes Duhaga (name of one of the kings of Bunyoro-Kitara).

CHAPTER THREE
SYLLABIFICATION IN WORDS

If you think that the word you are writing will not be finished before the line
on which you are writing is finished, you should decide on a syllable at
which to split it and then take the remaining part of it to the beginning of a
new line. The connection between its two parts must however be indicated

by a hyphen (-).
To do this successfully the knowledge of syllabification of the Runyoro-
Rutooro words is necessary.

Syllabification in words may consist of:

A. SYLLABIFICATION WITH VOWELS
|. SINGLE VOWELS

A single vowel (short), e.g.
e-mu ‘one’, i-ta-ka ‘soil’, a (exclamatory word)

2, DOUBLE VOWELS

A double vowel (long), e.g.

ce! ‘yes’ eee (exclamatory word)
aa-‘a! (exclamatory word)

4, DIPHTONGS

A diphthong (short), e.g.

ai-zi-re ‘he has come’
oi-rii-rwe ota? ‘how did you spend the day? Good afternoon’

el ‘which’ (cl. 9), ou ‘whom’ (cl. 1)

ft, SYLLABIFICATION WITH CONSONANTS

A single consonant (long), e.g.
n-te ‘it is a cow’, m-bwa ‘it is a dog’, n-do-ho ‘I am present’

--- page6 ---
’ Chapter three: Syllabification in words

_ CONSONANT + VOWEL

. consonant plus vowel (short), e.g.
e-ki-bi-ra ‘forest’, a-ma-ta ‘milk’, e-ki-si-sa-ni ‘picture’,

o-ku-tu ‘ear’

CONSONANT + TWO VOWELS

consonant plus two vowels (long), e.g.
e-kii-na ‘hole’, a-baa-na ‘children’, o-ku-lee-ta ‘to bring’, o-ku-tuu-ra
‘to unload person’

CONSONANT + DIPHTONG

consonant plus a diphthong (short or long), e.g.

a-mai-zi ‘water’, o-boi-ne ‘you have suffered much’, a-bai-ngi ‘many,
twins’, e-ki-te-teei ‘frock’

_LABIALISED CONSONANT + VOWEL

consonant plus w plus a vowel, e.g.

(a) o-mu-nwa ‘mouth, lip’, o-mu-twe ‘head) — short
(b) o-mwa-na ‘child’, o-mu-gwe-nyo ‘mange) — long

-LABIALISED CONSONANT + DIPHTONG

consonant plus w plus diphthong, e.g.

n-twai-re ‘I have taken’ n-dwai-re ‘ I am ill’

PALATALISED CONSONANT + VOWEL

consonant plus y plus a vowel, e.g.

(a) o-ku-lya ‘to eat’, o-ku-te-bya ‘to narrate’ — short
(b) o-ku-lye-rye-sa ‘to entice’, e-bye-gi-ra ‘strange things’ — long

A consonant plus y plus a diphtong, e.g.
m-byai-re ‘I have planted’, o-tyai-ze ‘you have sharpened’

C, SYLLABIFICATION WITH NASAL COMPOUNDS
1. NASAL COMPOUND + VOWEL

Nasal compound plus vowel, e.g.
e-nte ‘cow’, 0-ku-bu-mba ‘to mould’, e-nko-ko ‘chicken’
o-ku-ka-nga ‘to frighten’, a-taa-mma-nye ‘he/she did not know me’

2. NASAL COMPOUND + TWO VOWELS

Nasal compound plus two vowels, e.g.

e-ntee-ko ‘base’, e-ntii-ka-lya ‘patridges’
e-ntoo-le ‘water frog’, e-ntaa-hi ‘sparrow’
o-mmee-nye-re ‘so that you may pluck for me’

}, NASAL COMPOUND + DIPHTONG

Nasal compound plus diphthong, e.g.

e-mpai-ri-mi ‘male dog’, e-ntoi-jo ‘fresh butter’
e-ntai-ki ‘small hill of white ants’
tii-nnai-gi-re ‘I have not walked away aimlessly’

Nasal compound plus w plus vowel, e.g.

e-ntwi-ga ‘giraffe’, e-nkwa-si ‘wax’, e-ntwe ‘ear of corn
e-njwe-nge ‘tassel’, tii-mmwi-re ‘I have not shaved’

¢-mbya-rwa ‘edible fruit like peas’, e-mpyo-ra ‘scars’
0-taa-mmyo-ra ‘I do not twist me’

6. PALATALISED NASAL COMPOUND + DIPHTONG

Nasal compound plus y plus diphthong, e.g.

ti-mbyai-re ‘I have not planted’

tii-mmyoi-re ‘I have not twisted’

Note: All vowels before nasal compounds are pronounced long. But only the
length of the vowels before a nasal plus a nasal is indicated by doubling the
vowel.

D. SYLLABIFICATION WITH DOUBLE CONSONANTS
1. DOUBLE CONSONANT + SINGLE VOWEL

Double consonant plus single vowel, e.g.

i-bba-no ‘person or thing that causes a sensation’, e-bbi-ni-ka ‘kettle’
e-ki-bbi-ra ‘King’s court’, e-bba-ru-ha ‘letter’

2. DOUBLE CONSONANT + DOUBLE VOWEL

Double consonant plus double vowel, e.g.

e-bbee-se-ni ‘basin’, e-bbee-ru ‘banner’, 0-ru-bbaa-ho ‘board, bench’

3. DOUBLE CONSONANT + DIPHTHONG

Double consonant plus diphthong, e.g.

wa-rrai ‘sound made by a crying baby’

4, PALATALISED DOUBLE CONSONANT + VOWEL

Double consonant plus w plus vowel, e.g.
o-ku-bbwa-ku-za ‘to break so hard that noise is made’

E, CONSONANT PLUS APOSTROPHE

k’-o-boi-ne ‘I am sorry for you, you have suffered much’
n’-e-nte n’-e-mbu-zi ‘with cows and goods’

CHAPTER FOUR

WORDS AND AFFIXES

A. WORDS AS UNITS OF THE LANGUAGE

In speech as well as in writing no distinction is made between words and
affixes. All what we hear when a person speaks and see when he writes are

just words. This is so because affixes are component parts of words which

require them. They are never separated from them even in writing. They are
here presented as two separate entities only for the purpose of grammatical
analysis and study.

The study of words in this chapter is limited to the function which they fulfil
in speech, that is to the parts of speech to which each one of them belongs.
This knowledge is very essential because in writing a word for a particular
function in speech must be written separately from another word of a
particular function. It is due to lack of this knowledge that some Runyoro-
Rutooro writers experience difficulty in word division when they are writing.
lhe knowledge of the parts of speech to which the Runyoro-Rutooro words
belong will also help to tell which words require and which ones do not
require affixes.

Ij, PARTS OF SPEECH

(he Runyoro-Rutooro words may grammatically be divided into the
lollowing parts of speech:

1, Nouns

| hese are words which signify the names of things concrete or abstract, e.g.

lectero ‘Peter’; ente ‘cow’; enkoko ‘chicken’; entebe ‘chair’; amagezi
‘intelligence’; obutiini ‘fear’.

--- page8 ---
4 Chapter four: Words and affixes

). VERBS

‘hese are words which signify actions connected with nouns or the state in
vhich the nouns are, and are brought into concordial agreement therewith by
he subjectival concord, e.g.

Omuhuma naakama ente.

The herdsman is milking the cow.
Omwisiki naaruka ekiibo.

The girl is weaving a basket.
Enkoko niikooka.

The cocks are crowing.

Yozefu ali omunju.

Joseph is in the house.

Omwana aina ekijuma.

The child has a fruit.

3, ADJECTIVES

These are words which qualify the nouns, and are brought into concordial
igreement therewith by the adjectival concords, e.g

omukazi omurungi ‘A good woman’
embwa embi ‘A bad dog’
omusaija omuraira ‘A tall man’

But see Chapter sixteen on pp. 295ff.

1. PRONOUNS

These are words which signify things concrete or abstract without being their
ames, e.g.
nyowe ‘I’; itwe ‘we’; iwe ‘you (sg.)’; inywe ‘you (pl.)’; uwe ‘he or she’;
-0 ‘it, they’ as in: bo, gwo, yo, lyo, go, kyo, byo, etc.

5. NUMERALS

order. The numerals

These, too, qualify the nouns in terms of quality and
cordial agreement therewith by the

from one to five must be brought into con
aumeral concord, e.g.

omuntu omu ‘one person’; abasaija babiri ‘two men’.
ebintu bisatu ‘three things’; ebbakuli ina ‘four bowls’.
ente itaano ‘five cows’; amaju ataano ‘five houses’.

The stem ingaha? ‘how many?’ also takes numeral concords e.g.

abaana baingaha? ‘how many children?’
ebitabu bingaha? ‘how many books?’

Note: ente zingaha? ‘how many cows?’ instead of ente iingaha?

emiti ingaha? ‘how many trees?’ instead of emiti eingaha?

6. ADVERBS

These words describe the verbs with respect to manner, place or time; they
also describe adjectives or other adverbs with respect to manner. e.g.

Ohandiikire kurungi.

You have written well.

Onu mwegesa kwo mali.

This indeed is a true teacher.
Ndaakurora nyenkya.

I shall see you tomorrow.
Uganda neegenda omumaiso.
Uganda is progressive (lit. going ahead)
Ikaarra hanu.

Sit here.

Engeso ze mbi muno.

His behaviour is very bad.

/, IDEOPHONES

hese are basically adverbs. They describe the verbs, the adjectives or other
adverbs in respect of manner, sound, smell, action, state or intensity, e.g.

okununka cuucuucu ‘intensity with smell’
okwiragura siisiisi ‘intensity with being black’
okwera tiitiiti ‘intensity with being white’
okunuliirra nulinuli ‘tasting very sweet’
okusaarra peepeepe ‘tasting very bitter’
okwokya begebege ‘intensity with being hot’

okutukura tukutuku ‘intensity with being red’

Note that some Bantu grammarians refer to these joining words or particles
as prepositions or conjunctions, according to how they are translated in
English, (see also Rule 14 of the Runyoro-Rutooro Orthography). It should
be realised, however, that to call these words prepositions or conjunctions is
indeed to view a language through the medium of European
grammatical concepts. For the behaviour of the prepositions or conjunctions
in English is entirely different from that of what are called joining words in
Runyoro-Rutooro.

Examples of how joining words or particles are used and translated into
English are given in chapter seventeen, pp. 321ff.

(hese are the words which precede a negative and an affirmative sentence

expedient, therefore, that they should be regarded as an independent part of
speech. They are:

(1) Words of Negation
(a) Declinable

These are built on the root -aha which takes appropriate genitive particles to
mean that something or a person is not there. They therefore answer
questions about the presence of things, e.g.

Kyomya aroho aho?

Is Kyomya there?

Waaha, taroho.

No, he is not there.

Ente zaaha, tiziizire

No, cows have not come.
Karamoja ebyokulya byahayo.
There is no food in Karamoja.

When -aha takes the class 15 genitive particle kwa, it makes a blunt ‘no’,
e.g.

Byakisaka agenzire?

Has Byakisaka gone?

Kwaha, tagenzire.

No, he has not gone.

(b) Undeclinable
These are:
A’a: as in:
A’a, lekera aho kutalibaniza abantu.
Refrain from troubling the people, I do not want it.

Nangwa: as in:

Nangwa tindukugyayo!

No, I am not going there!

Nga: as in:

Nga, rugayo tindukukuteera.

No, come here, I am not going to beat you.

Naakataito: as in:
Tamanyiire kimu kusoma naakataito.
He does not at all know how to read.

B. Parts of speech 41

Naakake: same use as foregoing.

Busa: as in:

He has done nothing wrong.

Busaho: as in:
Busaho, tarukwijayo kunu.
No, he will not come this way.

Ntai: as in:
Ntai maau! twasanganankaha n’ogu!
God forbid, where did I meet him!

). NOUN PREFIXES

E. CONCORD PREFIXES OF THE NOUNS

All words relating to the noun (whether it be expressed or understood) must.
it. This is done by means of concord prefixes _

be brought into agreement with
attached to verb, adjective and numeral stems and to pronominal roots

E. Concord prefixes of the nouns 47

The concord used with verb stems and pronominal roots, is termed the

Pronominal Concord. That used with adjective stems is the Adjectival
Concord. That used with numeral stems is the Numeral Concord. Relative
pronouns may also be referred to as Relative Concords.

In the sentence ebinyonyi byombeka omumiti ‘Birds build in trees’ the P.C
on the verb byombeka is bi- ‘by-’. In omusaija mukuru ajwahire ‘The old
man is tired’, the adjective is mukuru. Its A.C is mu-. In ebitabu bibiri
ebya mugisa obite omukabada ‘Put Mugisa’s two books into the
cupboard’, the numeral is bibiri. Its N.C is bi-. In the sentence abantu
abarukubaza bali nkaha? ‘Where are the people who are talking?’. The
Relative Pronoun or R.C on the verb abarukubaza is aba-.

Note that instead of pronominal concords the first, second as well as the
third person singular are represented on the verb by Personal Prefixes, e.g. in
the sentences nsoma ‘I read’, tusoma ‘We read’, osoma ‘You read’,
musoma ‘You read (pl.)’ asoma ‘He/She reads’, the Personal Prefixes are
fie, tu-, O-, mu- and a-.

(¢) Demonstrative roots

I! the subject pronominal concords are attached to the roots -nu (-nuuna)
ind «li (-liimya), the words thus formed are called demonstratives. Those
huill up on the root -nu (-nuuna) refer to things which are close to the
speaker and those which are built up on the root -li (-liinya) refer to things
which are a distance away from the speaker, e.g.

nus omuntu onu ‘this person’

niitwe banu ‘It is we who are here’

nuwe onu ‘It is this one here’

nubo banui ‘It is these ones here’

eryato liri ‘that boat’
nuwe oli ‘It is that one’

abantu bali ‘those people’

amaato gali ‘those boats’
nubo bali ‘It is those ones’

(e) The modal particles -ta and -ti

-ta? and -ti are not pronominal roots, but in order to express their meaning a
subject personal prefix or pronominal concord must be attached on to them.
They are believed by many a Bantuist to be verbs without an infinitive prefix
just in the same way as verbs -li ‘to be’ and -ina ‘to have’ are. When a
subject personal prefix or pronominal concord is attached on to them, they —
express an adverbial concept. Examples of their use are given below:

Abakazi balima bata?
How do women dig?

Mukatugira muti, “Obu aliija mulituha ente.”
You said to us, “If he comes you will give us a cow.”

Patuuha ekagamba eti, “Mali mali mali, tingira waitu.”
Ihe crested crane said, “Truly indeed I have no relatives.”

‘THE INFLUENCE OF THE INITIAL VOWEL ON THE SUBJECT AND OBJECT PRONOMINAL
CONCORDS

Subject pronominal concords to which an initial vowel is added are used as
yubject relative concords, translated in English as ‘Who’, ‘Which’ or ‘That’,
according to the class in which the noun being represented is.

(bject pronominal concords to which an initial vowel is added are used as
object relative concords as well as demonstratives for things which are not
fur from the speaker, or which have been mentioned, or which are in the
inind at the moment.

I'xceptions are, however, to be found in the relative concords for nouns of
classes 1, 4 and 9 and the demonstratives for nouns of classes 1, 2, 6 and 12
is shown in the tables below'®

wecording to the class in which the noun being represented is.

Ihe following tables show the subject and the object relative concords as
well as demonstratives obtained from subject and object pronominal
concords respectively:

Ihe irregularity of the subject and object relative concords for classes 1, 4 and 9
in due to the fact that nouns with nasal class prefixes employ prominal concords
which differ from their own class prefixes. But it should be noted that ou, the
object relative concord for nouns in class 1 and ogu, the demonstrative for things
id for nouns in the same class are etymologically related. m is dropped
an initial vowel is added to the object pronominal concord -mu- to form the
word or the object relative concord ou. In the demonstrative for the things in
iind it is replaced by the letter g with the result that the word or the
istrative ogu is formed. Probably, the omission of the letter m in one case
iid its replacement by the letter g in the other is an attempt to avoid omu, a word
(hat would have been confused with omu, the word for one in English. For object
relative concords for classes 4 and 9 the case is slightly different. While the letter
in dro ee in the object relatives with the result that the word or the object
felative ei is formed, it is restored in the demonstrative. For demonstratives for
‘lunes 6 and 12 the irregularity is negligible, for age and ako are here used aga
anda a are used in Runyankore-Rukiga.

(4) Elision of the subject relative concord

The initial vowel of the subject relative concord is often dropped after the
noun it represents, unless emphasis is necessary, e.g.

Omukono gwatiire engoma tiguhunga nzige.
The hand which beat the drum (royal drum) does not catch locusts.

Iraro ligwirwe nyoko likwima enseko.
The madness which attacks your mother denies you some laughter.

Mpayo omukubi gundi, gunu tinkyagulya gwagwibwamu ensuhera.

Give me another relish as I can no longer eat this one because of the fly

which has just fallen in it.

Omwana wa bandi (w’abantu bandi) noomukambura aliire ki?
For what fault do you have to rip other people’s child?

Mpayo omukubi ogundi.
Give me other relish.

(5) Objective relative concords
(a) Orthographic convention

In spelling object relative concords stand as independent words before verb
stems, e.g.

Abantu aba yakozeseze baali bake.
The people he employed were few.

Omuti ogu yabaiziremu obwato akaguheera siringi ataano.
He paid fifty shillings for the tree out of which he made the trough.

Aga bagaya nugo gabutosa.

Water thought of as being relatively insufficient to stir is usually found

out to be too much for it.

Eki otakaliire toyebaarra.
Never put much hope in something you have not yet eaten.

l', Concord prefixes of the nouns 59

(b) Elision of the object relative concord

Note the omission of the object relative concords in the following sentences

and the apparent use as subject relative concords of the object relative
concords ou and ei on pp. 288ff.

I’mikeeka baarukire yoona bakagigura.
They bought all the mats they had woven.

Omuti yabaiziremu obwato akaguheera siringi ataano.
He paid fifty shillings for the tree out of which he made the trough.

(¢) Influence of the particle -a (often referred to as the -a of relationship) on

60 Chapter four: Words and affixes

Note the omission of the second vowel in spelling when -a of relationship is
added to a relative concord ending in a and also the irregularity of ezit + a.

But although with such relative concords -a of relationship is apparently not
represented in writing, the particles must always be pronounced as if it was
represented in writing, that is, they must always be pronounced long. For
example aba must be pronounced abaa; aga must be pronounced agaa; eza
must be pronounced ezaa; and aka must be pronounced akaa. This length
is, however represented by doubling the vowel when these genitive particles
relate a noun to a self-standing pronoun. See genitive particles used between
nouns and self-standing pronouns below.

(d) Elision of the particle -a

The -a of relationship or merely -a at the end of the particle is always elided
before another vowel. The elison, according to the Runyoro-Rutooro
orthography rules, must be marked by an apostrophe. But exceptions do~
occur. See Chapter 8 pp. 145ff.

4, RETENTION OF THE INITIAL VOWEL

They retain their initial vowel:
(1) In emphatic possessive pronouns

something out of the many things possessed e.g.

I have seen Bisereko’s book but not Baguma’s (Lit. The book I have seen —

is that of Bisereko; that of Baguma I have not seen it).

The front door is not shut (Lit. The door of the front of the house is not
shut)

My child is older than yours. (Lit. The child of mine is older than yours).

Tiese particles are generally referred to as copula, because like the English
popula ‘is’, they connect subject and predicate.

1. DISTRIBUTION OF THE PARTICLE ni-

The particle ni- is always used

(1) In questions

Before questions built on the interrogative root -ha? ‘who?, which?’ and in
answers to such questions, e.g.

Ayasire ecupa enu nooha? ‘Who has broken this bottle?’
Ni Byakutaaga. ‘It is Byakutaaga.’

Niinyowe. ‘It is I.’

Noonu. ‘It is this one.’

Omwana anyakurwaire n’ow’oha? ‘Whose child is sick ?’
N’owa Kabonesa. ‘It is Kabonesa’s (child).’

N’owange. ‘It is mine.’

Omumiigo enu yombi ogwawe niguha? ‘Which of these two walking
sticks is yours?’

Nigunu. ‘It is this one’

Niguli. ‘It is that one (a big distance away).’

Noogu. ‘It is that one (a little distance away).’

Note that here the words which the particle ni- precedes are normally proper
nouns, self-standing pronouns, possessives including proper nouns preceded
by genitive particles and demonstratives.

(2) With self-standing pronouns following nouns

Before self-standing pronouns coming after nouns. Here the particle is
needed to help the self-standing pronoun bring the noun it is representing
into prominence, e.g.

Ekyagwa kiki?

What is it that has fallen?

Kitooke.

It is plantain tree.

Kitooke nikyo kigirwe.

It is the plantain tree that has fallen.
Omungoye zinu zombi orurungi niruha?
Which of these two clothes is the nicer?
Runu. ‘This one.’

Nirunu. ‘It is this one.’

Runu nurwo rurungi (kukira oru).

This one is the nicer. (Lit. This one is the nicer)

I’, The particles ni- and n- 65

Here may be included self-standing pronouns representing nouns which are
not mentioned, e.g.

Ibara lyawe niiwe oha?

What is your name? (lit. Your name you are who?)
Niinyowe Rwanyabundindi (lit. I am Rwanyabundindi.)
Onu nuwe isenyowe.

This is my father. (lit. This it is my father)

Banu nubo bankoonyiire.

These are the ones who have helped me.

lt should be noted that the verb coming next to a self-standing pronoun to
which the particle ni has been added expresses a relative meaning even if no

relative concord is used. For information about this see p. 177, Nos. 8 and
0),

(1) Before verb stems in the present imperfect, e.g.

'nkooko niikooka. ‘The cocks are crowing.’
I'ntale neegamba. ‘The lion is roaring.’

Obviously demonstratives built on the root -li are not used with self-standing —
pronouns for the first and second person, both in singular and plural, to

which the copula ni- has been added. Instead, emphasis for the first person is"
expressed by demonstratives built on the root nu-, while that for the second
person is expressed by the demonstratives for classes 1 and 2, for the things
in mind. The meaning is, however, slightly different from that expressed
when nouns in classes 2 - 15 are in use. For example, expressions niinyowe
onu and niitwe banu in addition to meaning ‘It is I’ and ‘It is we’, respec-
tively, they also mean ‘It is I, I am here’ and ‘It is we, we are here’, respec-
tively. Cf. daranyowe and daraitwe on p. 73. Similary niiwe ogu and

there, pl.’ In many cases however, the expressions Niiwe ogu! and Niinywe
abo are used idiomatically to mean ‘Are you the person(s) I hear of?’

2. DISTRIBUTION OF THE PARTICLE -M- RESPECTIVELY N-

The Particle m- respectively n- are always used before demonstratives
(1) In response to a request, e.g.

Mpa omwigo gwange ogu.
Give me that stick of mine.
Negunu.

Here it is.

(2) In answer to the question where, e.g.

Ekitabu kyange kiraha?
Where is my book?

Nkinu.
Here it is.

Nkiri.
There it is.

Nkikyo.
There (rather near) it is.

(3) Merely to refer to something and the place where it is, e.g.

Aboojo mbali nibaija.
There are the boys coming.

Omwana ngugwo aizire.
There is the child, he has already left my place.
(Lit. There is the child, he has come)

G. THE PARTICLE KA AND THE JOINING WORD NA

|. THE PARTICLE KA

This particle has two shades of meaning. Before nouns, adjectives, self-
standing pronouns and demonstratives to which the particles ni- and n- have
been added, possessives, numerals and verb stems in the indicative mood, it
emphasizes the meaning expressed by the word it precedes. Thus, in the
example:

Ka muntu ‘the very person’

Ka murungi ‘a really good one’

Ka niiwe ‘it’s really you’

Ka wange ‘it’s really my relative’

Ka ngunu ‘here he/she truly is’

Ka batabajuma ‘they do abuse them’

lsefore a verb stem in the subjunctive mood it expresses apparent permission
\o do something, hence its being thought of as a shortened form of the verb
okuleka ‘to let alone, leave, abandon, stop, cease’. Thus, ka tugende ‘Let’s
yo’ and ka tuzaane ‘Let’s play’ have the implication of leka tugende and
leka tuzaane respectively.

(he following examples illustrate the use of ka before self-standing
pronouns and demonstratives to which the copula ni- or n- has been added.
!'xumples of the particle ka before other words may be found in section 22
of the Runyoro-Rutooro orthography rules.

(|) ka before self-standing pronouns to which the particle ni- has been added

ka niiwe ‘it is not any other person but you’
ka niinywe ‘it is not any other people but you’

2. THE JOINING WORD NA

This is discussed here because like the copula or the particle ni- it is often
used not only before nouns and possessives but also before self-standing
pronouns and demonstratives. Unlike the copula ni- which connects subject
and predicate, it normally joins one word to another, e.g.

Omwana na nyina
The child and its mother

Ente n’embuzi
Cows and goats

When it is not preceded by a noun it associates a person, thing (mentioned 0
represented by a self-standing pronoun) with unmentioned person ©
persons, thing or things connected with certain action or state, e.g.

G. The particle ka and the joining word na 71

Na nyina akaba aroho.
His/Her mother, too, was present.

Na uwe aligenda.
He/She, too, will go.

N’owaitu abarungi baliyo.
There are beautiful people in our area, too.

When it is preceded by a verb it associates a person or thing with a known
person or persons, thing or things in an action that was or that will be done,
6p.

Araija na ise.

He/She will come with his/her father.

Ndaagenda na iwe.
| shall go with you.

Unlike the copula ni- which in the orthography is separated before nouns
und possessives but joined to the self-standing pronouns, demonstratives and
the interrogative words built on the root -ha?. The joining word na is always
separated from the word it precedes. Care must, however, be taken not to
wonfuse the copula ni- and the joining word na. Compare the examples
below with those on pp. 65ff.

Only examples of how na is written before self-standing pronouns, and
hefore demonstrative are given below. Note the use of an apostrophe when
(he demonstrative it is preceding begins with the vowels a, e, and o. Trans-
lation puts into account both uses of na, that is when it begins a sentence and
when it is preceded by a verb.

74 Chapter four: Words and affixes

I. SOME NAMES OF RELATIONSHIP
1. GENERAL REMARK

The words ise, nyina, isenkuru, nyinenkuru, isenkati, nyinento,
nyinarumi, and isento are names of relationship. They express relationship
for the third person singular. In English they are rendered his/her, father/
mother/grandfather/grandmother/paternal aunt/maternal aunt/maternal uncle/
paternal uncle, respectively.

But, when they are added to appropriate self-standing pronouns they lose
their original meaning and become names of relationship for the person for
whom, or the thing for which, the self-standing pronoun to which they are
added stands for.

Thus, if the name ise is added to the self-standing pronoun nyowe ‘I’ the
word thus formed will mean ‘my father’. If it is added to the self-standing
pronouns inywe ‘you’ (pl.) and bo ‘they’ the words formed will mean ‘your
father’ (pl.) and ‘their father’, respectively. If it is added to gwo ‘it,
mongoose’ and yo ‘they, mongooses’ the words formed will mean ‘its
father’ and ‘their father’ respectively.

The table below shows how names of relationship for the third person
singular combine with an appropriate self-standing pronoun to express
relationship for the person or thing for which the self-standing pronoun with
which they are combined stands for. Note the implication of the compound
status’ of the names isenkuru, nyinenkuru, isenkati, nyinento,
nyinarumi, and isento and also the absence of a class prefix on all names of
relationship. Names of relationship are further discussed in Chapter 7 on pp.
114f.

IMPORTANT NOTES:

) The Runyoro-Rutooro Orthography Rules make no reference to the
riting of names of relationship. But since rule 11 enjoins that “two or more
ords forming one noun shall be joined together”, it follows therefore that
mes or relationship which are formed by the addition of the names of |
lationship for the third person singular to an appropriate self-standing -
onoun must also be written as one word. Thus we have isenyowe, isiitwe,
iinywe and isebo; isenkurunyowe, isenkurwitwe, isenkurwinyowe and
enkurubo, etc.

) The words iba, isezaara and nyinazaara are also names of relationship |
r the third person singular. They mean ‘her husband’, ‘his/her father-in-
w’ and ‘his/her mother-in-law’, respectively. Like the other names of
lationship which refer to the thir d person singular, when they are added to
propriate self-standing pronouns they also lose their original meaning and
come names of relationship for the person for whom the self-standing
onoun to which they are added stands for

) The words kamaanya ‘self-willed person’, imira ‘person people have a_
ywror of (from okumira ‘to swallow’) and nyaabura ‘narrow-minded
‘rson’ are used before self-standing pronouns for the first and second
rson singular and before demonstratives for things in mind. In writing,
ey must be separated from the self-standing pronoun or the demonstrative’
ey precede. They are by a bereaved or fear-stricken person, or by a
srson who is expressing pity for another person, e.g.

kole ki hati kamaanya nyowe? (Used metaphorically to mean that if a
isfortune has happened to me, I am such a self-willed person that I even do
xt know where I can take refuge).

gyaha imira iwe? ‘Where will you go? A misfortune has happened to you:
-ople have a horror of you’

J. Adjectival concords — K. Numeral concords 77

Deero nyabura ogu hati akole ki? (Meaning a misfortune has happened to
him. He is like a narrow minded person. What will he do now?)

J. ADJECTIVAL CONCORDS

Unlike prominal concords which in certain classes of noun differ from the
prefix of the noun they stand for, all adjectival concords must take the form
of the prefix of the noun which the adjectival stem to which they are
attached qualify. Thus, if the adjectival stem -rungi ‘good’ is to qualify the
noun omukazi ‘woman’ it must take the prefix omu- so that it becomes
onmmoraig, With this adjective we can now say Omukazi omurungi ‘a good

woman’ or Omukazi ogu murungi ‘that woman is good’.

Adjectival roots are given on p. 43 and p. 295. By using appropriate noun
prefixes on pp. 44f as many adjectives as the roots can allow may be made.
Note that unlike in English where an adjective precedes the noun it qualifies,
djectives in Runyoro-Rutooro follow the nouns they qualify.

The root -ki? ‘What?’ takes adjectival concords, but it does not qualify the

noun at all. See p. 52. For other words which qualify nouns see Chapter 16,
pp. 296ff.

Kk, NUMERAL CONCORDS
|, CARDINALS

(iumeral 1-5 are brought into concordial agreement with the noun they

(2) Structural peculiarities

Numeral concords of the numeral stems 1-5 are the same in form as the
pronominal concord except for classes 1 and 6.

In mere counting, the numeral 1 takes the numeral concord for class 9;
numerals 2-5 take the numeral concord for class 10, e.g. emu ‘one’, ibiri
‘two’, isatu ‘three’, ina ‘four’, itaamo ‘five’.

To express the number of times, something has been done the numerals 1-5 ©
take the adverbial prefixes li- (di-) and ka-; -mu takes li- (di-, e.g. dimu
‘once’. The rest take ka-, e.g. kabiri ‘twice’ kasatu ‘thrice’, kana ‘four
times’, kataano ‘five times’.

An alternative to this is by means of the words omurundi ‘time’ and
emirundi ‘times’ used before a numeral which in case of the numerals 1-5
must be brought into concordial agreement with the word in use, e.g.

omurundi gumu ‘once’, emirundi ebiri ‘twice’, emirundi musanju-
‘seven times’, emirundi ikumi ‘ten times’, emirundi ikumi n’ettaano
‘fifteen times’, emirundi makumi abiri ‘twenty times’.

This will normally follow the Interrogative -ingaha? ‘how many?’
preceeded by the word emirundi in a question inquiring the number of times
the action has been done. The verb employs the tense prefix ka, e.g.
yakakuteera emirundi ingaha? ‘How many times has he/she struck you?”
yakanteera emirundi ebiri ‘He has struck me twice’. But see Note on p. 80,

Numerals 6-9 and all the multiples of 10 are nouns each of which ig
identified with its particular class. Details about them are given below:

10 is a noun of class 5 with its plural in class 6, e.g. abasaija ikumi ‘ten
men’, abakazi makumi abiri ‘twenty women’.

A unit is connected to the tens by na which is subject to sound change. In s )
doing, however, the numerals 1-5 keep their concordial agreement, e.g.

abaana ikumi na babiri ‘twelve children’
ameezi ikumi n’asatu ‘thirteen months’
ebiro makumi abiri na bitaano ‘twentyfive days’

kK. Numeral concords 719

The plural of ikumi ‘ten’ is used up to fifty. From sixty up to a hundred, the
classes 9 and 7 prefixes are used, e.g.

40 (a)makumi ana
100 (e)kikumi

30 (a)makumi asatu
70 (e)nsanju

80, 90 and 100 being nouns of class 7 have their plural in class 8 e.g.

Ebyenda ‘nineties’
Ebikumi bisatu ‘300’
Ebikum bitaano ‘500’

Ebinaana ‘eighties’
Ebikumi bibiri ‘200’
Ebikumi bina ‘400’

600, 700, 800 and 900 take the class 11 prefix with no plural e.g.

(o)rukaaga ‘600’
(o)runaana ‘800’

(0)rusanju ‘700’
(o)rwenda ‘900’

|,000 takes the class 11 prefix with its plural in class 10 up to 5,000 e.g.

(o)rukumi rumu ‘1000’ ~—— (e)nkumi ibiri ‘2000’

6,000, 7,000, 8,000 and 9,000 take the class 13 prefix with no plural e.g.

(a)kakaaga ‘6000’ (a)kasanju ‘7000’
(a)kanaana ‘8000’ (a)kenda ‘9000’

10,000 may be named as omutwaro gumu or akakumi kamu. The former
is a noun of class 3 with its plural in class 4. The latter, is a noun of class 13
with its plural in class 14.

100,000 may be named as emitwaro kikumi or akasiiriira kamu. The latter
ii 4 noun of class 13 with its plural in class 14.

1,000,000 is named akakaikuru kamu and therefore, a noun of class 13
with its plural in class 14.

When counting the initial vowel, on the multiples of 10 up to 1000, is
omitted e.g.

ente nkaaga ‘60 cows’

embuzi kikumi ‘100 goats’

abaana bikumi bina ‘400 children’

abahi b’omusoro rukaaga ‘600 tax payers’
amiaju rukumi ‘1000 houses’

Por 2000-5000 the initial vowel is maintained e.g.

emiti enkumi ibiri ‘2000 trees’

SECTION B - OBJECT RELATIVE

Rule 13

The object relative shall be written separately from the subject prefix of the verb
it precedes.

Examples

ekitabu omwana ogu eki arukusoma kyange

(the book which that child is reading is mine)
omuhyo omwana ogu yampa ngunu

(here is the knife which the child has just given to me)
omubazi nyina ogu amuhaire gumuraizeehoga kurungi

(the medicine which his/her mother gave him/her made him/her feel better)
omuntu ou orukurora (the person you are seeing)
abantu aba oboine (the people you are seeing)
abakazi aba aleesire (the women whom he has brought)
abantu aba naboine (the people whom | saw)
motoka aba aeraatwara (the people whom the motor car will take)
ékitabu eki omuhaire (the book which you have given him)
eki otamanyire (what you do not know)
emiti ei otemere (the trees which you have cut down)
amanyire obwire obu araagenderamu (he knows the time at which he will go)
akasumi aka oraagenderamu (the time at which you will go)
akasumi aka yagendiiremu (the time at which he went)
aikale nambere ali (let him stay where he is)
nambere ndi harungi (where | am is a good place) |

Note: The object relative does not precede a noun, the genitive particle is used
instead:

ekitabu eky'omwana ogu arukusoma kyange.
(the book which that child is reading is mine).
omuhyo obw'omwana yampa ngunu
(here is the knife which the child has just given me)

SECTION C - PREPOSITIONS AND CONJUNCTIONS

Rule 14

Except for “omu" and "aha" prepositions and conjuctions shail be written
separately from the word they precede. Where elision occurs when they
precede words beginning with a, e, 0, (or words in the li-ma class [Class 5]
beginning with i) the separation shall he indicated by an apostrophe.

Examples
ali owa Yozefu (he is at Joseph's house)
Kato na Isingoma (Kato and Isingoma)
na nyowe muboine ( have seen him too)
na iwe oOraija (you also will come)
na uwe araagenda (he also will go)
kora nka so (do like your father)
ensi nka Uganda (a country like Uganda)
agiire ow'‘omunyoro (he has gone to the chief's home)
embuzi n'entaama (goats and sheep)
baza nk’abandi (speak like others)
akamucumita n'icumu (he stabbed him with a spear)
nikyangasana rk'izooba (it shines like the sun)
okwezi n'izooba (the moon and the sun)
kisongoire nk'icumu (it is pointed like a spear)
habw'okugonza (for love)
habw'ekitiinisa (for honour)

Exceptions:-

habwange (for me, because of me)
habwawe (for you, because of you)

“omu" and "ha" shall normally be joined to the word they precede.
Examples

omu: enkende ziikara omukibira (monkeys Stay in forest)

SECTION D - POSSESSIVES AND THEIR PARTICLES

Rule 15

Possessives shall be written separately from the word they precede or follow. Where
elision occurs when they precede words beginning with a, e, o (or words in the
li-ma class [Class 5] beginning with i) the separation shall be indicated by an

apostrophe.

Examples
ali omunju ye (he is in his house)

omulimo gwe mwangu muno (his work is very easy)

omwana wa Peetero (Peter's child)

embuzi ya Ruteebemberwa (Ruteebemberwa's goat)

ibara lya mutabani waawe (the name of your son)
amaiso g'akaana kato (the eyes of a little child)
emirimo y'omunyoro (the duties of a chief)
omusoro gw'ebyenyegesa (education tax)
omutunzi w'ebitabu (bookseller)

akaba wa ikumi na babiri (ne was the twelveth)
orwigi rw'embaaho (wooden door)

muSaija w'embabazi (he is a kind man)

amaizi g'okunaaba (water for washing)

abantu ba kunu (the people of this place)
abantu b'owaanyu (the people of your country)
mubiro bya hati (nowadays)

rmubwire bwa nyenkya (in the morning)

abantu ba Uganda (people of Uganda)

irembo ly'iguru (the gate of heaven)

ekisoro ky'omumaizi (aquatic animal)
amaranga g'izooba (sun beams)

omugaso bw'icumu (the use of a spear)
amaani g'ihe (the forces of an army)

omuntu w'okubanza (the first man)

omwojo wa mukaaga (the sixth boy)

orukaarra rwa ikumi na ina (the fourteenth line)
ekitabu kya kabiri (the second book)

SECTION E - ADVERBS AND CONJUNCTIONS

Rule 16

Adverbs and conunctions shall be written as one word.

Examples
Habwokuba (because habwaki (why)
noobwakubaire (even if) nkooku (as)
habweki (therefore) kakuba (if)
nambere (where) noobu (even if) |
obundinoobundi (sometimes) nahabweki (and therefore)

SECTION F - COPULA

Rules 17 to 19

the Affirmative copula ni shall be joined to the pronoun it precedes.

Examples
niinyowe (it is |) nugwo (it is it)
niiwe (it is you) nuho (it is there)
niitwe (it is we) numwo (it is in)
nuwe (it is he) nooha? (who?)
niinywe (it is you) nibaha? (wo are they?)
nubo (it is they) noogt (it is that one)
nooli (it is that one over there) noonu (it is this one)

Note: This rule does not apply if ni precedes a noun.

Examples
ni Marko (it is Mark)
ni munywani wange (it is my friend)

If the noun following the copula "ni" begins with a vowel, the separation shall be
indicated by an apostrophe.

Examples
Ekitwara omuhumakati mpora n'obusinge
(It is peace that makes a lady walk slowly)
Rumpaire kinu n'Anna
(It is Anna who gave me this)

21. Adverbial Suffixes
Adverbial suffixes "ho", "mu", "yo" shall be joined to the words they qualify.

Examples

bakagendayo (they went there)
tukahikaho (we arrived there)

"KA" and "KWO"

"Ka" shall be separated from the word it precedes, "kwo" from the word it follows.
In the case of "ka" before a word beginning with a, e, or 0, the separation shall

be indicated by an apostrophe.

Examples
ka niiwe (it is you) ka niinywe (it is you)
ka niinyowe (it is |) ka niitwe (it is we)
ka nuwe (it is he) ka nubo (it is they)
ka nungi (how nice it is) ka mahano (what a wonder)
ka batali nubo (itis not they) - k'atali nyowe (it is not 1)
ka mmuboine (I have seen him) ka muboine (what a pity)
k'oboine (what a pity). k'akamurora (he actually saw him)
mazima kwo (truly) k'akanyeeta (how fat he is)
muntu kwo (he is truly a person) k'aije (let it come)

a

Rule 23

Geographical names should be spelt according to the above rules. Changes in
spelling of important place names should be gazetted.

Examples

Tooro Butiiti Toonya Butoobe Kiijura

PART IV - FOREIGN WORDS

Rule 24

Foreign words which are in common use shall be written as they are commonly
pronounced by the Banyoro and Batooro.

bbulangiti = blanket sukaali = sugar
ebbeeseni = basin ekooti = coat
efulaano = flannel esaati = __ shirt
Omungereza = English . kamera = camera
Omufaransa = French sente = cent
Omucaina = Chinese sooda = soda
peeterooli = _ petrol busiki’ = whisky
bbururu = blue Sande = Sunday
etikiti = ticket bbaasi = bus
stookingi = stockings piida = P.W.D.
motoka = motor car gavana = governor
gavumenti = government simenti = cement
kaliya = Carrier stoovu = stove
kampuni = company stoowa = store
saagini = sergeant spaana = Spanner
Ofiisi = Office stampu = stamp
esweta = sweater kalenda = calendar
etaulo = towel steeringi = steering wheel

Note: Authors are expected not to use a foreign word normally when appropriate
Runyoro-Rutooro word can be found.

PART V - REPORTED SPEECH

Rule 25

A comma or a colon may be used before quotation marks if desired, and the words
quoted shall be introduced by a capital letter.

Examples

Mukuruwentebe yamugarukamu ati, "Ekigambo kinu nikiija kuhanuurwaho obundi"
(The chairman replied, "This matter will be discussed later on"

Abaserukale baagira bati: "Titumurozire hamaiso"
(The policemen said: "We did not look at his face"

PART VI - THE PARTICLE "NYA"

Rule 26

-The particle "nya" shail be joined to the noun it precedes.

Examples
nyamukazi (the woman)
nyamusaija (the man)
nyabuzaana (the female slave)
nyakaana (the child)
musaija nyaisija (he is really a man)
mukazi nyaikazi (she is really a woman)

PART VIl - NAMES OF PEOPLE

Ruie 27

As names of people have many legal implications. their spelling must be left to the |
individual concerned. For the purpose of meaningful writing. however, book writers
should spell such names according to how they are pronounced by the

Banyoro and Batooro.
